FLORENCE IS
DECLARED THE
SOLE HEIRESS
Gypsy Blythes'Claims
Are Dismissed.
SHE GETS FIVE MILLIONS
VERDICT FROM SUPREME COURT
OF THE UNITED STATES.
Final Decision Has Been Rendered
in the Long and Bitter Litiga
tion Over the Big
Estate.
The highest tribunal in the land, the Su
preme Court of the United States, has
given its final decision in the Blythe liti
gation, and it seems that Florence Blythe
Hinckley is at last secure in the posses
sion of the fortune left by Thomas H.
Blythe precisely sixteen years ago, for
his death took place, on April 4, 1883.
The decision was in the suit of John \V.
Blythe and Henry T. Blythe, known as
â â¢tin- Kentucky" or "Qypsy" Blythes.
against Florence Blythe Hinckley, and. as
reported in a telegram received by the
defendant yesterday afternoon from
Washington, P. â¢'-, recites that "the
Blythe appeal has been dismissed on the
authority of the cases formerly cited.
Chief Justice Fuller to-day delivered an
opinion holding that the decisions of the
State court disposed of the constitutional
and treaty questions and of the objec
tions respecting section IH7S of the United
Btatee revised statutes." This decision
was tjiven on motion of Attorneys John
Qarber and Robert Y. Haynes to dismiss
or affirm a summary process that waives
argument in court and, disregarding the
court calendar, brines the case up for de
cision on "law and motion day"â the first
Monday in the monthâ thus saving many
weary months of litigation.
The property left by Thomas H. Blythe
at his demise In lss.'i was estimated to be
worth $6,000,000. It consisted chiefly of
San Francisco real estate, including the
famous Blyihe block on Market and
Geary streets and Grant avenue, and it
produced a revenue of more than $15,000
a month, and this is the fortune of which
Mrs. Florence Blythe Hinckley becomes
possessed through the decision of the
United States Supreme Court. The at
torneys' fees .in the litigation have been
heavy, but the income of the estate has.
it is said, been sufficient to defray them.
Blythe died a bachelor, but there were
several lurid episodes in his career on
which colorable claim was made to a
share in his estate. When the litigation
over the property he had accumulated
was fairly under way, there were no less
than thirty-one claimants to portions of
it the chief claimants being Alice Edith
Blythe. who set up a claim as the mil
lionaire's widow, and Florence Blythe,
now the widow of Frederick W.. or
"Fritz." Hinckley. Alice Edith's claim
went glimmering under the sledge-ham
mer blows of the many attorneys who
took part In the litigation, and Florence's
attorneys downed one opponent after an
other until by this last decision her
claims stand incontrovertible. She is
the daughter of a woman whom Blythe
met in London while she was still un
married, and the courts have held that
he recognized Florence as his daughter.
She came to this country on Blythe's
death, when she was but 10 years of age.
and, shortly after attaining her majoriiv,
became the bride of "Fritz" Hinckley,
son of the well known foundry man. Mr.
Hinckley died of pneumonia a year or so
I ago.
The "Kentucky," or "Gypsy," Blythes'
claim was based on the supposition that
Thomas H. Blythe died without issue and
that inheritance therefore vested, through
I common ancestry, in John W. and Henry
'T. Blythe of Kentucky. They maintained
, that Thomas H. Blythe was of Gypsy
I ancestry, though he ignored that fact on
! coming to this country, and that his near
est kin are the descendants of a Gypsy
i Blythe who settled In Kentucky many
! years ago. Their latest contention has
j been that an alien of British birth can
not Inherit, there being no treaty to that
effect with Greal Britain, and that Flor
ence Blythe being an alien at the time of
Thomas Blythe's death and for more than
the statutory limitation of rive > ears |
I thereafter, could net legally claim the i
dead millionaire's fortune. The words of
the telegram, "holding that the decision
of the State court disposed of the con
stitutional and treaty question," would
seem to settle this matter "once and for
i all.
But Attorney Edmund Burke Holladay,
who has the Interests of the "Kentucky"
Blythes in charge, refuses as yet to be
lieve that the new decision is all-compre
hensive. "Wait until we see the full text
of the decision." he said last night, "and
then we shall know."

T. Fortman of thp Alaska Packers' Asso
ciation joined the circle and the new
comer was notified that a story was ex
pected of him. There seemed to he an im
pression that the gentleman was not pre
pared, but h<~ was. In fact he had a good
one, especially as it was fresh and could
be verified by a number of gentlemen
well known in legal and commercial cir
cles.
Mr. Fortmnn was one of a party of four
hunters who left this city last Saturday
evening and reached their destination sev
eral hours later. The place was Grand
Prairie, twelve miles from Elmira. The
party consisted of Justin F. YV. Henshaw
of the Supreme bench, W. <; Henshaw of
the rnion Bank of Oakland, Judge E.
Nusbaumer of Oakland, and Mr. Portman.
The hunters were prepared to slay the
geese that are at present destroying the
growing grain of the farmers of" that lo
cality, and from all accounts they had all
the fun they desired. They stayed over
night with Doc Stewart and Abe Krumo
and under the guidance of their hosts
found themselves In the pits or blinds in
the great expanse of valley at an early
hour the next mornine. Judge Henshaw.
Mr. Fort man and Abe Krumh occupied
three of the pits, and ahotit a mile away
W. G. Henshaw. Judge Xusbaumer and
Doc Stewart hid themselves in similar
places. Surrounding the pits were a lot
of live and dead decoys to attract the
birds, hut better than the decoys were the
goose calls of Stewart and Krumb.
As soon as it was lipht the geese began
to fly, and such a varietyâ Ross gpesp,
snow geese, Mexican, grey, cackling
Hutchlns, grey brant and <<th<rs. They
came in bunches, flocks and swarms and
right merrily did the humors kfep up a
shower of Xn. fi shot upon the flying hirds.
When the time came for the hunters to
start for the train a count-tip showed that
Mr. Fortman and Judge Henshaw had
bagged W geese of various kinds. While
Judge Nusbaumer and W. G. Henshaw
had tumbled over lfiri geese. At last ac
counts their hosts were still picking up
birds that had "died out of hounds." and
had increased the lot by about fifty.
"I tell you It was great sport," remarked
Mr. Fortman.

COMPETITION IN LIGHTING.
AT the meeting of the Supervisors' Street Com
mittee on Thursday it was decided in the near
future tc discuss a plan for fostering competi
tion in lightiog the streets, public buildings, stores and
residences of the city. Dr. Perrault introduced a reso
lution at a meeting of the board a few weeks ago
which provides for placing all competitive companies
upon a : equality. It grants every lighting
the privilege of erecting poles outside
the fire limits and .enacts that all wires within the
fire limits shall be placed underground forthwith.
The Street Committee decided to request representa
i the Merchants' Association to meet it in joint
conference for a discussion of the subject, and such
a meeting will be held to-morrow afternoon at 2
o'clock.
It may not be generally known, but it is a fact
nevertheless that no franchise is necessary for the
construction and operation in this city of lighting
The constitution lays down the process in
unmistakable terms. Section 19 of article XI reads
as foil
In any city where there are no public works
owned and controlled by the municipality for sup
plying the same with water or artificial light any
individual or any company, duly incorporated for
fuch purpose under and by authority of the laws
of this State, shall, under th* direction of the Super
'ntendent of Streets, or other officer in control there
,nd under such general regulations as the mu
may jnvscribe for damages and Indem
nity for damages, have the privilege of using the
public streets and thoroughfares thereof, and of lay-
Ing down pipes and conduits therein and connections
therewith. s<> far as may be necessary for introduc
ing into and supplying such city and its inhabitants
âº ither with gaslight or other illuminating light, or
with fresh water for domestic and all other pur
3, upon the condition that the municipal govern
ment shall have the right to regulate the charges
thereof.
Supervisor Perrault's resolution is the general reg
ulation which the sbove section declares shall be
prescribed by the municipal authorities. But whether
it is necessary or not is an open question. Judge
Seawell has decided that under this provision of the
organic law no restriction can be placed upon com
petitive lighting companies by the Supervisors. Un
doubtedly if the board should fail to prescribe the
regulations referred to competing concerns could go
on and prescribe them for themselves. No Board
cf Supervisors or Common Council would have the
power to defeat competition in lighting by failing
to act.
These things are interesting in view of the recent
â  organization in this city of an independent electric
light company. That concern will have no*-difficulty
whatever in introducing a competitive plant. We
doubt whether, under a fair construction of section
19, the Board of Supervisors could forbid the erec
tion of poles so long as poles are used for any electric
wires, telegraph or telephone. The purpose of the
constitution is to reduce lighting to a competitive
basis, and we do not believe that process can be pre
vented by stifling competition as has been done here
tofore.
Some of the soldiers who were brought back from
Honolulu to be discharged and are still here make
what appears to be a reasonable complaint. They
say they were denied travel pay on the ground that
Honolulu is in foreign territory, and are denied the
..extra pay allowed for service in foreign territory
on the ground that Honolulu is as thoroughly do
mestic as Milpitas. Uncle Sam ought not to set up
a surething game like this.

It. was hardly necessary for a Coroner to hold an
inquest over a man at Antioch who had died after liv
ing ninety-three years. The suspicion of old age was
so well founded that it required no official confirma
tion.
Easter Sunday in many parts of the East was
marked by a blizzard of more than ordinary severity,
and the bonnet of the day will have to go over for a
month and make its debut at the May day picnics.
Vanderbilt has a right to give his son $10,000,000,
of course, but there are many worthy American
fathers who decline to bestow such wedding gifts,
and yet the young people get along all right.
South Dakota can be proud of its soldiers living
and its soldiers dead. No finer body of men went
through this city on the way to war, and none more
valiantly ever met the foe.

musical car. Not even for the sake of boosting art
by the display of good clothes would it attend the
opera.

side.

PRESSURE OF A CZAR.
\A R. C. P. HUNTIXGTON displays bravado
I \ to a degree unequaled by any other Ameri
can capitalist. He has the courage not merely
of his convictions, but of his interests. He has ar
rived in San Francisco with his vestibule train and
with his retinue of ten servants, and has instantly
assumed a tone of arrogance that no conqueror of
ancient times or Oriental despot of the' present cen
tury could have excelled. He stigmatizes the people
of the State who resisted and defeated his proposal
to fund the Central Pacific debt for a hundred years,
at the rate of 2 per cent per annum, as "turbulent,"
and defiantly announces in relation to the debt itself
that "it is from the patrons of the road, largely Cali
fornians, that the money must come, as the road has
no other means of earning it." The fact was antici
pated, but not that it would be presented in the form
of an edict or a ukase.
The funding bill, as originally proposed, was
beaten not by a small faction, but by the almost
unanimous action of the people of California. There
was a strong desire, which still exists, for peace and
co-operation with the railroad, if consistent with the
preservation of our liberty and our independence,
and but for the treacheries of the last campaign and
of the closed legislative session an era of harmony
might have been already inaugurated. There is still,
as there has always been among citizens who repre
sent industry, enterprise and capital, no inclination
to prolong the strife that unquestionably does play
into the hands of demagogues. But the dogmatism
and the dictatorial expressions of Mr. Huntington
appear to indicate that he no longer recognizes the
right of the inhabitants of California even to call their
souls their own.
It is a pity that this venerable railroad capitalist,
who has impressed his individuality upon every en
terprise with which he is connected, should abso
lutely fail in ordinary respect for his fellow citizens
and should seriously regard himself as the Czar of
California, if not of the Union. It may be true that
the patrons of the Central Pacific will have to pay
its debt, but it is also true that the collateral proper
ties and the private fortunes that the railroad people
have extracted from the toil and sweat of our citizens,
and that, too, by diversions of revenue and appro
priations of capital and income that would not be
sanctioned in any honest court of equity, would
cover at least six or eight times its amount. It is
barely possible, though not probable, that this coun
try may drift into imperialism, and that Mr. Hunt
ington and those who act with him may acquire in
law the position they now usurp in fact. But unless
this deplorable transformation of our institutions oc
curs the time is near at hand when effective ways and
means of resisting railroad exactions and railroad
tyranny will be devised. The successors of Mr.
Huntington at least are likely to pay some attention
to the voices of American communities.
THE SYNDICATE MfINIA.
WHILE the press, the statesmen of the country
and, to a large extent, the general public
have been more clamorous and more em
phatic than ever in denouncing trusts and condemn
ing their operations, the creation of such combina
tions has been carried forward to an extent unparal
leled in any other age or in any other land. The en
terprises of Hooley and other promoters in Great
Britain are insignificant in comparison with what has
been accomplished here.
The center of activity in the movement has been
of course in New York, but most of the combina
tions have been organized under the lax laws of
New Jersey and are nominally corporations of that
State. It is stated that in 1898 the companies author
ized there and in West Virginia aggregated a capi
talization of only a little less than one thousand mil
lions of dollars; and the Philadelphia Manufacturer
says the indications are that this year the New Jersey
authorities will authorize corporations or syndicates
whose shares will at par value aggregate six times
that enormous sum.
In fact, the organization nf syndicates, or trusts, to
control the manufacturing industries of the country
has become a mania among Eastern capitalists, and
many of the more conservative journals of that sec
tion of the country believe it has been carried too
far. The fear is that the shares of many of these
great combinations have been sold at speculative
prices owing to the large amount of idle money in
the financial centers, and that sooner or later there
will come a collapse which will be injurious not only
to the speculators but to the country at large.
From a compilation of the opinions of the financial
and industrial press on the subject recently made by
the Literary Digest it appears there has been no
lack of words of warning to the public. The Finan
cial Chronicle is quoted as noting that in January
and February of this year there were definitely
formed new combinations having an aggregate capi
tal of $1,106,300,000, and saying: "When the end
will come no man knows definitely, but that it will
come everybody of any foresight realizes perfectly
well. He knows, too. what the end will be when it
does come, and whoever can take in his sail during
the next few months will be a Vise mariner."
Bradstreet stated several weeks ago: "It would
seem that in a good many cases' the common stocks
represent more or less water. For the time being,
however, the financial public seems to be infatuated
with the movement, and though even a cursory ex
amination of the situation does not fail to develop
the fact that it possesses elements of weakness, there
is nothing to negative the presumption that, like
everything of the kind, it must run its course."
It is to be regretted that this mania for speculation
in syndicate stocks has broken out among the capi
talists of the East just at a time when the country has
every natural prospect of an era of unexampled pros
perity. If the men of the East would use their sur
plus capital in exploiting and developing the vast
rich regions of the West and South they would confer
an immense benefit upon the country and reap a rich
profit for themselves. Moreover their ventures, if
made with ordinary sagacity, would be safe from
risks of disaster. There would be none of that dan
ger which thoughtful men now see impending over
Wall street and its speculators.
Fortunately, the appetite for battle news, whetted
by the Philippine campaign, does not suffer from
famine during the lull in Asia. The Chicago cam
paign keeps up the supply of gory news, with a
larger percentage of fatalities than in the Philippines.
The Carter Harrison faction is doing most of the
benevolent assimilation.
There are disadvantages attached to fame. Corbett
and his wife cannot have a quiet little family row
without news of. it being flashed across the continent.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 4, 1899.
THE LAW AND THE GAMBLERS.
WHEN the Supervisors in response to the de
mand of the public enacted the ordinance for- ,
bidding gambling at Ingleside, the track man
agers, the poolsellers and their touts were loud in
their boasting of a pull with the courts that would j
enable them to defy the law. They are less boastful j
now. Their pull has been found wanting. The !
courts have upheld the law.
The opinion delivered by Judge Murasky in dis
solving the injunction issued to restrain the police â 
from entering the racetrack, and which was pub- j
hshed in The Call on Sunday, merits more than a
passing notice. It is a clear exposition of the law
governing the point in controversy and emphatically
upholds the right of the police to enter the grounds
of the racecourse and arrest law-breakers who may j
be found there.
After pointing out that the plaintiffs in the case
(the gamblers) assert .their innocence and allege that i
the action of the defendants (the police) is damag
ing to their business: while the defendants allege that
the plaintiffs are wrongdoers and violators of the law,
Judge Murasky said:
Where greater injury will be inflicted upon plaint
iff than upon the defendant by the dissolution of an
injunction pendente lite, the injunction should be
maintained until the final hearing. Where the con
trary result will occur the injunction should be dis- !
solved. If the plaintiff's allegations are true it will
suffer much loss, unless defendants are restrained,
and defendants will suffer none by the injunction.
If, however, the allegation of defendants is true, that i
plaintiff is a law-breaker, then the injury to the !
municipality from the continuance of the injunction ;
would be greater than any that can be compensated â 
by money, and tVierefore greater than^ any the
plaintiff can suffer.
That statement of the law is in accord with reason
and justice. The injury that may be done to the
gamblers by the police is as nothing in comparison
with the wrong done to the community by the pool- j
sellers. As the Judge well said, the latter injury ;
would be "greater than any that can be compen
sated by money." The injunction therefore was dis
solved.
Commenting upon the decision, Attorney McEner- j
ney is quoted as saying he believed the decision of i
Judge Murasky ended the controversy. As the right
of the club to seek relief in a court of equity rested J
solely, according to the opinion of the court, upon a
question of fact, which was easily established in favor
of the police, there is no escape for the club under
the anti-pool ordinance, and it must either refrain
from further selling or suffer the consequences.
There can be little or no doubt that this opinion |
will prove correct. Racing, poolselling and track
gambling of the kind that have been so pernicious
at Ingleside have been driven from the great chic?
of the East, and it is not to be supposed that Cali- I
fornia law is less effective than that of her sister
States.
RELAXING EXCLUSION.
IT is reported that communication between the Chi
nese Minister at Washington and the Chinese
Consul at Honolulu is to the effect that a new
trentj- with China is made necessary' by our appear
ance in Hawaii and the Philippines, and that therein
the strict exclusion features of our present treaty will
be softened and relaxed. During the discussion of
Hawaiian annexation The Call declared that this
policy would be inevitable if we acquired tropical
possessions with the expectation of getting commer
cial value out of them. White men cannot work in
the plantations of Hawaii! The climate bars them
out. Without coolie labor there, the production of
the islands will decline to the zero where it stood
when reciprocity made sugar of sufficient value to
warrant the importation of Asiatic coolie labor.
When annexation occurred it was given out that our
Chinese exclusion laws would be extended and our
non-contract and other labor laws would speedily
follow, so that equality in the kind and cost of labor
would exist between the islands and the continent.
It is evident that this policy collides with the com
mercial expectations which induced annexation, and
that it is withdrawn and the planters are to have lib
eral treatment in respect to their labor supply, so as
to avoid that lack of laborers which would first cause
a substantial increase in wages, to the lowering of
1 rofits, and finally the disappearance of a consequen
tial surplus of production.
Our practical homogeneity of climate and physical
conditions has heretofore simplified the problem of
labor and wage?. There has been a fair equality of
hours, rights and wages in all parts of the United
States. As we enter the tropics this condition ceases.
Our people demand commercial advantages to offset
the cost of conquest and government. Commerce
cannot be without the surplus productions of labor.
Therefore tropical commerce depends upon that form
of labor suited to the climate, which must be in some i
way servile. Our home labor laws cannot apply to i
servile labor, for with their application its servility !
ceases and without servility its usefulness and profit j
to the employer cease.
These facts give color to the report of a new treaty !
with China, which will be a novelty because it will
continue restriction as to our continental possessions,
but relax it on our newly acquired islands. Therein ;
will first appear the inequality of law required by in
equality of physical conditions, and it will be official
notice served on American labor that it has no place
in the tropics.
Other departures from our domestic system will of
necessity occur in quick succession.
It is evident now that the influence of the Hawai
ian planters put the code of laws for those islands i
to sleep on the files of the last Congress. They de- ;
sire to entrencfh coolie labor in a new Chinese treaty, !
which can be easier ratified by the Senate than a law j
to secure the same end can pass both houses of Con- I
gress.
The victory so far is distinctly with those who j
want -cheap labor. They refuse to submit to equality
of law and wages. In the logical development of the
issue their competition with the products of our do
mestic labor will finally cause its employers to clamor
for equality. As the employers in our tropics hold j
the whip hand and refuse to permit their labor and
wages to be leveled up, will not the final result be
that our domestic labor and wages will be leveled
down ?
In popular government inequality is odious and
finally yields, as slavery did to freedom, which be
came the universal law of the republic. Now there
is a change of ideals. That was an age of senti
ment; this is one of commercialism, and will its spirit
permit a higher level of labor and wages in. the tem
perate than in the torrid zone?
There are reasons for believing the insurrection in
the Philippines has been virtually broken, but now
a new strife appears between the Turks and the Bul
garians, and the war chorus continues to hold the
stage.
Huntington's statement that the Southern Pacific
Company had nothing to do with the Senatorial con
test at Sacramento imposes upon Herrin the duty of
explaining what he was doing there.
THE TRUTH ABOUT THE
ATHERTON INTERVIEW
Some three or four weeks ago an Interview with Gertrude Ather
ton was obtained by the representative of a reputable Eastern syndi
cate and sold to the leading papers of the United States, including The
Call. For some reason, known only to herself, the authoress repudi
ated parts of this interview and said it was garbled and attributed to
her ideas and words which she had never uttered. A venomous little
afternoon paper published in this city gave this repudiation to the pub
lic, piecing it out with sentences that betrayed -Its own spitefulness and
imbecilit -. In due time thi; reflection upon the truthfulness and ac
curacy of his work reached the gentleman who gave the Atherton in
terview to the press. His reply, which is here given, is in many re
spects as interesting as was the interview that caused it to be written:
150 Nassau Street.
NEW YORK. March 29. 1599.
Editor The Call. Ban Francisco. Cal.: In reference to Gertrude Ather
ton's statement, as printed in the paper you sent me. I have to say that her
assertions are not based on facts. Why she selects The Call to discharge
her venom upon, I do not know: for the New York Journal printed the mat
ter, by mistake, on the Thursday previous to its publication in The Call, in
stead of on the same date, and the Philadelphia Press and Chicago Tribune
simultaneously with The Call's publication.
I was in correspondence with Mrs. Atherion subsequent to the publication
of tho interview of which she complains, and she had no criticism whatever
to make regarding it. Her statement that she wired me for a proof of the
interview is made from whole cloth, as the records of the telegraph offices
will show if any one cares to look it up.
I enclose you the second article, which Mrs. Atherton refers to as a "dull
jumble of words." The editor of the Saturday Evening Post of Philadelphia,
who printed it. was kind enough to write me that he considered it the work of
a master hand. I fancy the Post is as good a judge of English ard of in
terest as Mrs. Atherton.
Now. as regards the interview in detail. I had never seen Mrs. Atherton
previous to the meeting in Washington. T must confess that I have read
none of her books. I noticed a statement in a New York paper that she
was making a study of the Tnited States Senate for the purpose of writing
a novel. I wrote her that I would like to talk to her about her observa
tions and several other things. I did not refer to one of. her books, because,
as I say, I have never read nnc. \
I went to the boarding-house where Mrs. Atherton lived in Washington
with the distinct and understood purpose of talking with her for publication
and nothing else. Mrs. Atherton well understood this, because she was kind
enough with her own hands to bring a table to me upon which I might take
notes.
Mrs. Atherton says: "In order to make a spicy article he made me say
things regarding the Senate and members that came straight out of his imag
ination." I am sorry to say that Mrs. Atherton seems to have a very failing
memory in this respect. I should not have made the statements if they had
not been made by her. Had it been my desire to make a spicy article, as she
states. I might have included therein her remarks concernine: her own
marital experiences, her reference to her husband as the late "unlamented,"
and a few other matters of that sort.
In reference to Washington society Mrs. Atherton's strictures as given by
me were exactly what she said. I realized the importance of absolute accu
racy, and made no statement whatever which was not hers verbatim. T
n.ight possibly have included Mrs. Atherton's criticisms of the society she
met in the place in which she lived, or of the callers to whom she denied
herself while I was th^re. but that seemed to me unnecessary -
Referring again to my second article from her. T have to say that about
the only statement she makes which is correct is that I sent a proof to her.
She returned that proof with full permission to me to publish it. and the only
correction she made was in the heading. This proof and the letters which
have passed between Mrs. Atherton and myself on the matter can be pro
duced if necessary, though I must confess I have no inclination to make a
correspondence with a lady public.
In conclusion, the situation seems to me to be this: A question of veracity
between Mr?. Atherton and myself. The greatest point of difference is that I
can prove what I say. while Mrs. Atherton has thus far confined herself to a
tirade against newspapers in general and me in particular.
The fact is that Mrs. Atherton's criticisms of "Washington .society and
of several of the Senators have furnished food for discussion, and that .some
of this discussion has not been as altogether peasant to Mrs. Atherton as it
might have been. The refuge she takes in denial has been sought by others
who have talked for publication and been criticized. Personally, this happens
to be my first experience. It is the first time I ever interviewed a woman. I
am inclined to think I will end the chapter as I. began itâ with Mrs. Atherton.
You are at liberty to publish any or all of this, if you care to, over my
signature. Very truly, CHARLES CULVER JOHNSON.
DAN POLK, SELLS
NAME AND FAME
DAK POLK, banjoist. Bohemian, brother to the only Willis, lineal de
scendant of James K. Polk, one time President of these United States,
and well known in club, society and musical circles of this city, has
sold his name. $
Sold not only his name, but his fame as well.
Together they were knocked down for the unprincely sum of twenty dol
lars (S2O 00).
Think of purchasing a Polk, a pedigreed virtuoso of the fjr=t rank in ban
jodom. for one beggarly, yellow double eagle of the realm: It sounds like a
smoker's vision, and yet it is trueâ sadly, incontestably true.
Dan. the pet plunker of the swim and the vaudeville's pride, has sold out.
And now another long-haired youth of nimble fingers and dreamy eyes
smotes the banjo that Dan used to smite, looks the languishing looks that
Dan used to look, receives the flowers and salary and matinee notes that
once were Dan's, and wears in print and in private the proud name of a
President's descendant.
The once prominent Polk is now probably an ambiguous Smith or an
evasive Jones, lost in the shadow of a new name and the building of a new
reputation. The new Polk who separated himself from a score of honest dol
lars to become the same, is at the Orpheum. Read the programme for your
self. There, in tall, stout type, is emblazoned "Polk and Rollins, Celebrated
Musical Artists."
Polk and Kollins are the names of the famous banjo team founded and
perfected by Daniel. It was known and respected the length and breadth of
the vaudeville world for well-clad, parted-in-the-middle gentility and tri
umphant technique on the five staccato strings of America's national instru
ment.
But Dan. eccentric Bohemian that he is. wearied of the opulent fortunes
of a vaude villain, of a name historic, of a publicity national, and sold them
all out for the price already mentioned to an ambitious pupil of his who
hails 'from Kansas City.
What the purchaser's name used to be nobody seems to knowâ but now
It is Polk and paid fr>r. The same old Polk and Kollins lithographs decorate
the cigar stands and deadwalls of the city, with the hair and features of
Dan still intact. But a new boy. who only partly lives up to the beauty of
the posters, plunks where Dan once plunked and receives Dan's dinner and
boudoir invitations.
MODERN MACHINERY FOR
NEW LIGHT COMPANY
A. M. HUNT GOES EAST TO STUDY
BEST METHODS.
Work of Laying Underground Wires
Will Commence in a Short
Time.
A. M. Hunt, the well-known electrical
engineer, left this city on the 6 o'clock
train last night for an extended tour In |
the East. Mr. Hunt's mission is one of j
interest to the people of San Francisco, !
and, as San Francisco is the business j
center of the State, incidentally to the |
whole State. Mr. Hunt has been com- j
missioned by the Independent Light and i
Power Company, incorporated March 29 I
by Claus Spreckels and others, to make a
general Inspection of electrical plants
throughout the East. Regarding his jour
ney Mr. Hunt says:
"It will be my mission to investigate
light and power plants everywhere and
select the best methods from each of
them. In this way we can safely promise
that San Francisco will have the finest
plant in the world. The great trouble
with most plants is that they grow from
small beginnings. Wherever such is the
case you will find a piece of antiquated
machinery running side by side with the
newest and most improved. The inevit
able result of this is an 'increase In the
cost of production.
"Take the local plant, for instance. It
started a number of years ago on a small
scale. Of course all machinery it put
in at the time was the most modern, and,
as a matter of fact, all the machinery it
added as the plant grew has been modern,
but all the old. cumbrous machinery must
be taken out and an entire new plant put
in before they will have an up-to-date
plant, as greater strides have been made
in the lines of electrical machinery than
any other.
"The new concern will not start small
and grow. A complete plant of the new
est machinery will be put in, and this
will enable us not only to give better
service than the older companies, but we
will be able to do it cheaper.
"I have not yet made any definite plans
as to what cities I shall visit, but I will
?tnp off at Chicago, then probably go on
to New York, Philadelphia. Boston and
other cities. I will first make a study
gt the underground system of distribu
tion, as we intend laying the wires first
There are several such systems now in
use. chief among which is the Edison
system, where the wires are laid in rigid
ducts and a system of loose ducts. With
the Edison system it is necessary to tear
up the streets to replace wires, "but with
the other system old wires can be taken
out and new ones substituted from regu
lar stations without a pick being placed
In the ground or a paving stone moved
I am somewhat favorable to the latter
system, but I desire to make a comDlete
and thorough investigation.
"As soon as an underground system
has been chosen work will begin We
will employ an army of men tearing ud
the streets and laying wires. Of course
this is the most tedious work and we

GRAVE CHARGES
AGAINST THE
GIRLS' HOME
Alleged Cruelties to
Inmates.
DARK CELL AND HANDCUFFS
LAURETTA HALL COMPLAINS TO
THE SHERIFF.
Many Serious Accusations and a
Plea for Release From the Al
leged Chamber of
Horrors.
Sheriff Martin was made the recipient
yesterday of a very serious complaint
against the management of the California
Girls' Training Home at 147 Natoma
street. The complaint, or rather the
charges, were made by one of the inmates
of the home, a young girl named Lauretta
Hall, whose father and mother are now
on trial in the Superior Court on charges
of embezzlement. She is 16 years old, of
good character, and her presence In the
home is due to circumstances connected
with and growing out of the arrest of her
parents and not because of waywardness.
The girl declares that the inmates of
the home are subjected to treatment that
would not be permitted or tolerated in
any penal institution in the State. She
says they are given insufficient and poorly
cooked food; that it is coarse in quality
and so unpalatable as to be nauseating.
She also avers that the inmates, who are
all young girls under 18 years of age. are
not permitted sufficient air and exercise
to keep them healthy.
It was in connection with the punish
ment given those who break the over
strict rules governing the conduct of in
mates of the home that Miss Hall had the
most serious charges to make. For any
slight infraction of the rules of the place
the guilty one is given what is called a
"check." When the girl has six of these
checks against her she is made to eat her
meals standing in the presence of other 3
who are seated at the dining room table.
The meal so eaten consists of plain bread
and water.
According to Mips Hall'? statement a
room, known as the "punishment room."
is set aside for those found guilty of more
serious infractions of the rules. Th":
windows of this room are boarded up,
leaving the interior as dark as it is pos
sible to make a room. There is no bed
in the apartment, those who are thrust
in there having to be content with a soil
ed mattress, which is thrown upon the
fl oor in one corner. Those who are put
in this room are fed on bread and water
and are kept there several days at a
time. Miss Hall says that the more re
fractory wrong-doers are frequently
handcuffed in order to make the dark
room punishment more severe. She cites
one instance in which the unfortunate cil
prlt. a girl named Mabel Morris, remained
handcuffed for three days. When she
was finally released her wrists were badly
swollen and discolored and she was un
able to use her hands for days afterward.
When Miss Hall was sent to the home
about six weeks ago she weighed 131
pounds. She now weigh? 105 pounds, and
declares her belief that she will soon die
unless she is taken away from there and
placed where she can have light and air
and proper food.

- " â â â 
The big turntable at Geary and Mar
ket streets, the property of the Geary
Street, Park and Ocean Railroad Com
pany, will remain where it is, at least
for the present. Judge Bahrs yester
day decided the injunction proceedings
instituted by the railroad company to
restrain Superintendent of Streets Am
brose from proceeding: under an order
of the Board of Supervisors and re
moving the turntable, the chancellor
holding that in the action at bar he
had no power to determine whether or
not the turntable was a nuisance in
fact. In consequence a permanent in
junction was granted, forever restrain
ing the defendants in the action from
removing the turntable by reason of
the direction to that eitect contained in
the resolution passed by the Board of
Supervisors.
The opinion is as follows:
The court in this action, as will be
hereafter shown, has not the power to
determined whether the so-called Geary
etreet turntable is a nuisance and
should therefore be abated. It is per
haps to be lamented that this question
could not have been squarely litigated
between the railroad company and the
people in this action ana a decision
reached which would have been a
finality.
The first question the court in this
E.'ticn is called upon to decide is whether
the Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, by a mere res
olution, can condemn as a nuisance and
* direct the removal of the turntable con
structed and maintained by the plaintiff
tinder, claim of legislative sanction and
franchise.
The Hoard of Supervisors on August 15,

Street Improvement Company may per
form its contract. And the Clerk is
hereby directed to advertise this resolu

On August 22, 1S!>8. this suit was com
menced to restrain such threatened re
moval of the turntable, and the Presid
ing Judge of this court issued a tempor
ary restraining order. The section of the
franchise under which plaintiff claims
the right to maintain the turntable is
V- follows, to wit:
"The track and all switches or turn
tables for said railroad shall be construct
ed under the supervision of and to thÂ«
satisfaction of the Superintendent of
Streets and the Street Committee of the
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco." If the turn
table was authorized by and constructed
pursuant to the provisions of the above
section of plaintiff's franchise, the turn
table has the sanction of the Legislature,
end car not be a nuisance in law. To this
effect is section 34*2 of the Civil Code,
viz. : "Nothing which is done or maintain
ed unuer the express auuiority of -a stat
ute can be deemed a nuisance."
Plaintiff contends that the turntable
was construcini as nr vi<jpn ov Â«=â¢.,' i rraÂ«
chise, with the consent and approval of
the officials designated therein; defend
ants contend that certain ordinances reg
ulating the construction of turntables
were violated, wherefore said turntable
is a nuisance, notwithstanding the ap
proval of the designated officials: plain
tiff denies the violation of these ordi
nances, but contends that they were all
coi.iplied with. â :â  !
The turntable had been, in fact, main
tained for six years before the resolution
rf the Board of Supervisors condemning
the same as a nuisance was passed. It
Is under these circumstances, conditions i
and contentions that the court is called '
upon to decide whether the Board of Su-
T>pr\-!8ors in thus by resolution sum
marily declaring the turntable a nuis
rr.ee and directing its abatement exceeded
Its p.'wprt
Defendants in their brief contend:
"An act of the Legislature of California, i
approved Apirl 25, 1883, conferred power
upon the Board of Supervisors of the City
and County of San Francisco to authorize
and direct the summary abatement of
nuisances. Statutes of 1*63, 6540."
The statute, referred to is in the fol
lowing langauge:
"The Board of Supervisors of the City
and County of Ban Francisco shall have ,
power by regulation or order:
"First.
"Second. To authorize and direct the
summary abatement of nuisances; to
make all regulations which may be neces-
Fary or expedient for the preservation of
the public health and the prevention of
contagious diseases; to provide, by regu
lation, for the prevention and summary j

â¢ piERCE electric CO., 5
Â© v V^\-;.v 11 11 820 Market street (opposite ' Palace Hotel), a
? DR. PIERCE, Inventor. SAN FRANCISCO. A
removal of all nuisances and obstructions
{P. the streets, alleys, highways and pub
-1 ? Â£ rounds ot said city and county; and '
i LJ? TGV ? n $ or regulate the running at
!, rge Â° f dogs and to authorize the de
struction of the same when at large con
trary to ordinances."
The power given by this statute over I
nuisances and obstructions in public
streets, highways, etc.. is by "regula
on ' Regulation is something entirely
distinct from resolution or order. By this
statute some nuisances may evidently be
abated by resolution or order, but the
power to abate them in public streets is
limited to "regulation."
ihe word "regulation" has a well-de
nned meaning in law. Perhaps the best
definition is that to be found in the Amer
ican and English Encyclopedia of Law,
volume 20, page 732:
'â To adjudge by rule, method or estab
lished mode; to direct by rule or restric
tion; to subject to governing principles
or laws." .
A power to "regulate" does not properly
include a power to "suppress" or pro- 1
hibit," for the very essence of regulation
is the existence of something to be regu
lated. But the power to "regulate a
trade, business, etc, authorizes the mu
nicipality to confine the exercise of such
business to certain localities, to certain
hours of the day, etc.
The Board of Supervisors under this
statute has not the power arbitrarily to
say that any man's house is a nuisance
because of Its peculiar construction or its
height, or that certain display signs are
nuisances because they project beyond
the property line to any extent. But the
Board of Supervisors has the power to
regulate within reasonable limits the
height of houses and the extent to which
signs may project, and they have the
right to enforce such regulations. As to
turntables, the same ' rule applies. The
Board of Supervisors clearly has the pow
er to prevent the construction of any
turntables In the future, providing they
.jo so by "regulation": It also has the un
doubted power, notwithstanding such a
regulation, to grant a franchise to lav a
turntable thereby and to that extent re
pealing the regulation. But if such fran
chise IS once given, the board has no
power during the life of the franchise to
retract the privilege.
A regulation means a law to which all
can conform. The resolution complained
of in this suit is clearly not a rule of con
duct, but a declaration of condemnation
concerning a special thing.
Regulations must be laws binding upon
all persons, and the Board of Supervisors
has no power of making regulations ex
cept by ordinance. In City of Denver vs.
Mullen. 7 Col., 345, the court says: "A
provision In the charter of a city confer
ring authority upon the Council" to make
regulations to secure the general health
of the inhabitants, 'to declare what shall
be a nuisance, and to prevent and abate
the same.' will warrant the declaration by
general ordinance of what shall constitute
and the conditions and circumstances un
der which a given thing shall become and
be deemed a nuisance, but does not au
thorize the Council, by mere resolution or
motion, arbitrarily to declare any par
ticular thing a nuisance which has not
theretofore been pronounced to be such
by law. or so adjudged by judicial deter
mination."
In Yates vs. City of Milwaukee. 10
Wall, 497. the Supreme Court of the United
States said: â â¢â¢" - â  ,\
--"But the mere declaration by the city
of Milwaukee that a certain structure was
an encroachment or obstruction did not !
make it so. nor could such declaration I
make it a nuisance unless it in fact had j
that character."
In Spokane Street Railway Company vs. I
Spokane Falls, 6 Wash., 521. the court I
said:
"There was a resolution passed by the
Council, but that resolution was a mere
special direction in this particular case. '
The law contemplates that In all such
cases there shall be a general ordinance i
which shall be a law of the city which it f
is bound to follow as much as the people j
are bound to obey. In an absence of an I
ordinance regulating such matters the j
city should have resorted to lerr proceed
ings and not taken In its own hands by j
force the execution of a decree against I
a party who has no legal notice of the
pendency of the matter."
Decisions to th effect might he multi- '
plied, but no useful purpose would be sub- I
served.
Aside from these general considerations i
that the city cannot ( act by order or reso
lution when its power is limited by
statute to regulation by ordinance, there
are other considerations which of them
selves would be sufficient to stay the con
templated action of the city.
Neither does the power to abate nuis
ances warrant the destruction of valued ,
property which was lawfully erected or I
anything which was erected by lawful I !
authority. It would indeed be a danger- j
ous power to repose in municipal corpora- !
tions to permit them to declare, by ordi
nance or otherwise, anything a nuisance
which the caprice or interests of those
having control of its government might i
see fit to outlaw without being responsible
for all th"c consequences, and, even if such I
power is expressly given by the Legisla
ture, it is utterly' Inoperative and void i
unless the thing Is in fact a nuisance, or
was created or erected after the passage
of the ordinance and in defiance of it.
* â¢ â¢ Therefore, except in cases of great
public emergency, when the emergency
may safely be regarded as so strong as to
justify extraordinary measures upon the
ground of paramount necessity, or when
the use of property complained of is so
clearly a nuisance as to leave no room for'
doubt* upon the subject, it is the better
course to secure an adjudication from the
courts before proceeding to abate it.
This doctrine was laid down in the Spo
kane case already cited.
"It is very questionable whether in any
such case where there is a doubt as to
whether or not there may not be exist
ing rights in the person whose property
is sought to be destroyed the municipal
corporation should proceed to its de
struction without resort to a tribunal
which may determine the right of the
matter. â¢ â¢ â¢ "While .a well-constructed
street railroad may be a technical nui
sance, if unauthorized, it is not danger
ous to either life, health or property, and
so long as the courts are open to the de
termination of such controversies there
is no call for violence or any manner of
arbitrary or oppressive action."
As Indicated in my opening sentences,
the court has not the power to determine ,
in this litigation whether the so-called
Geary street turntable Is a nuisance or
not. " The defendants have not filed a
cross-complaint asking such adjudica
tion, and any attempt on the part of the
court to so adjudicate would be void and
outside of the issues. This was express
ly held in the case of Easton Passenger
Railway Company vs. Easton, 133 Pa.
St. 505. The court says:
"The defendants have no right in this
proceeding to ask this court to settle any
question affecting the rights of the city
in the matter in controversy. They have
not filed a cross-bill, nor have they ap
plied to this court or any other court to
adjust the differences between the city
and the appellant company. They have
no prayer for relief. On the contrary,
they have assumed to decide the delicate
questions involved for themselves and to
enforce their decision by the strong
hand. This cannot be permitted. We are
of opinion that their acts were unlaw
ful and that the plaintiff Is entitled to i
the relief prayed for."
I am therefore of the opinion that the
permanent injunction prayed for by the
plaintiff should be granted to the extent
that defendants be forever restrained '
and enjoined from removing the said
turntable by reason of the direction to
that effect contained in the resolution
complained of.
Nothing in this opinion is intended to
enjoin said defendants or any person
specially damaged by reason of the ex
istence of said turntable from commend
ing a proper action at law or in equity
looking to the condemnation of said turn
table as a nuisance.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRTL 4, 1599.
FLED SLAVEDOM
TO THE MISSION
THERE is a wailing and a gnashing of t^eth throughout all Chinatown;
there's been many a punk stick burned since yesterday morning's sun
broke through the recking smells and smoke, and many a parley held
in the odorous, fume-thickened alleys, and every wire that works an
influence between the haunts of the Celestials and the office of Chief of
Police Lees has been stretched to the point of breaking in an effort to have
little Yue Sin, the favorite of the beau monde of the district, returned to the
brothel -whence yesterday morning she fled to the protecting shades of the
Mc-thouiet Mission.
Since the day she arrived here under false papers, just one year ago, Yue
Sin has been the stellar attraction of the gay youth of Chinatown. Three
months before she left Hongkong she was sold by her own mother. Her
father, who was a worker In the rice fields, died when little Yue Sin was
only eight years old. The wage of the rice gatherpr is no larger than pro
vides his living, and he left nothing. Yue Sin's mother struggled against pov
erty and recurring sickness until the one daughter she had saved from "the
basket" was just 18, then she succumbed to her needs and to custom and sold
her into the life she was to lead in this country.
A year ago she was brought here by Moun Dong, one of the most infa
mous slave dealers of Ross alley, and her alleged husband, Chung Wing.
She was placed in a rooming house at S4 Hoss alley. Despite the fact that at
once she became a favorite of Chinatown's half world she only waited an
opportunity for escape, but it did not come until just before daybreak yester
day morning, when her keeper fell asleep.
Then she unlatched the door and stole out into thfÂ» darkness of the alley.
At the end of it she found Officers Ben Castle and William Armstrong, and
into their hands she put a letter from a Christianized Chinese of Hongkong,
asking the first American official into whose hands it should come to assist
its bearer out of slavery and to the Methodist Mission <>n Washington street.
The two officers at once took her to Mrs. Lake, the kindly matron of the
mission, where she told her story- She begged piteously for relief from the
life she had been forced to lead, and Mrs. Lake, after the fashion of the la
di'-s of the mission, responded by communicating at once with the Society
for the Prevention of Vice. The society reported to the Police Department,
and Officer I. Tuchler of the California street squad was detailed to go with
a search warrant to the rooming house at S.K Ross alley to get the girl's be
longings.
He called at the mission for Tue Sin. who was to accompany him and iden
tify hor things, and accompanied by Mrs. Lake they worked th^ir way
through a menacing crowd of Chinese that news of the escape had gathered.
The doors of the rooming house were slammed in his face, but with a resound
ing kick of his well heeled boot Tuchler disposed of the barricade and led the
plave girl to her rooms on the top floor of the building. There she found a
portion of her things, though her silks and her money had been abstracted
from her trunks at the first alarm.
Then Tuchler led his charge hack through the hallways, which hn<l bp
come crowded with a muttering, threatening horde nf Chinese, down through
the reeking alley and back to the pure air of thr- mission, where, Mrs Lake
says, she shall stay until she can be disposed of in a way that will redound
otherwise than to her ruin.

CONFIDENCE IN THE BANK.
San Luis Obispo Money Is Pouring
Into the Commercial.
A week or ten days ago the State Bank
Commissioners granted to the Commercial
Bank of San Luis Obippo permission to
resume business, the institution having
been obliged to close Its doors at the time
of the failure of the County Bank. As
soon as the permission was granted the
officers of the bank called a meeting of
those interested, and with one accord
they promised to do all in their power to
put the bank upon its feet again
I'nder the agreement the doors were
opened yesterday and the success met
with was so encouraging that the offi
cers sent a dispatch to the Commission
ers to the effect that more money had*
been received that day in the shape of de
posits than on any day since it first went
into business. The Comisisoners regard
this as an evidence of the confidence of
the people of the county in the future of
the institution and general prosperity of
that locality.
A Family Disturbance.
Richard Harvey, 36 Alemany street,
was charged by his half-brother, W. J.
Sharp, before Judge Graham yesterday
with disturbing the peace. They occupy
the upper and lower fiats of the house
and Harvey, having been appointed ad
ministrator of his mother's estate or
dered Sharp to vacate, which Sharp re
fused to do and had Harvey arrested
The evidence was long and contradictory
and the Judge dismissed the case telling
them to take their troubles to the Super
ior Court.
Had a Bicycle Bell.
Ivan R. Lang, the bicyclist who was
arrested Sunday at Market and Sixth
streets for riding a bicycle without a
bell, proved to Judge Mogan yesterday
that he had the bell attached to his fin
ger, as is often the practice with bi
cyclists, and the case was dismissed. The
bicyclist who knocked down the two
girls, Louisa and Alma Miller, has not
yet been discovered, Lang being able to
prove that he was not the man.

SAVED FROM
DEATH BY A
VIGILANT CLERK
Two Boys Have a
Narrow Escape.
ARE ALMOST ASPHYXIATED
ONE LAD ACCIDENTALLY TURNS
GAS ON.
After Being Dragged From Their
Boom the Youths Are Resus
citated, but Not Without
Much Trouble.
Two boys named Ben McQuirk and
Donald McVlckera had a narrow escape
from death by gas asphyxiation yester
day morning.
Unmindful of parental warning the
boys went to Shell Mound Park, where
they drank considerable liquor. After re
turning 1 to this city they coxitinued their
libations until both were almost helpless
ly drunk. Rather than confront their
parents they decided to engage a room in
a lodging-house at 675 Mission street.
Some time after they had retired the
clerk of the house detected the odor of
escaping- gas, and he proceeded to make
an investigation. Suspecting: that the boys
had "blown out the gas," as he ex
pressed it, the clerk went to their room
and rapped for admittance. Failing to
get a response he burst open the door and
was almost overcome by the escaping
fluid. After hastily raising the windows
he dragged the unconscious boys into the
hallway. Then, thinking that they were
about to die, the clerk summoned the po
lice officer on the beat, who sent In a call
for thÂ« Receiving Hospital ambulance.
While waiting for the ambulance the
policeman, assisted by the clerk, carried
the boys downstairs. McVickers soon re
vived and begged not to be taken to the
hospital.
"Please don't take me to the hospital?"
pitifully remarked the unfortunate
youth. " "I would rather have died in the
room than to have my poor mother know
of my narrow escape. If she finds out
that I was so nearly asphyxiated I know
it will break her heart."
McQuirk, who is a messenger boy, also
recovered in a short time, and. like his
companion, pleaded not to he taken to
the hospital. As the doctor who was in
charge of the ambulance which arrived
a few minutes later was convinced that
the boys had recovered fmm the effects
of the gas they had inhaled the police
man took them to the City Prison, where
they were charged with being drunk.
McQuirk seemed to be in a stupor and
begged to be allowed to go to sleep..
<V I don't know how it happened," he re
marked when trying to explain how the
gas was turned nn. "Aft^r renting the
room I fell on the bed, first telling: my
companion to turn off the gas. It was
only a short time afterward that I was
awakened by the smoll of escaping gas,
and although I realized that we were in
danger of being asphyxiated I did not
have sufficient strength left to assist my-
Belf or McVickers. While I could not
hl'c-.ik I was extremely happy when the
clerk burst open the door and dragged
me out into the fresh air."
On being, told of the narrow escape of
the boys from occupying slabs in the
Morgue, Captain Spillane. convinced that
they had entirely recovered from the ef
fects of the liquor and gas. ordered them
released on their own recognizance.

NKW YORK STOCK MARKET.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, April 3.â There was a decidedly
, strong tone in the wheat pit at the start. May
j opened %'g'ic higher at from 7?.%c to 73% c
and during the first few minutes trading ad
vanced to 73% c. The strength at the opening
was as a consequence of an official report from
Missouri estimating the crop condition at 66
in that State, a drop of 14 points from the
condition a year r.go. The weather through
out the Northwest was still unseasonably cold,
but there were predictions of springlike weath
er for to-morrow. Under the Influence of a
prospective modification of the temperature
(â peculators forgot all about accomplished dam
age and they soli on the advance. As the ses
sion progressed visions of s] routing seeds an.l
budding trees rose in the minris of traders and
heavy liquidation set in. Tins selling changed
the market from the extreme of buoyant light
ness to heavy depression. May whÂ«at slipped
off quickly to 71 Â«c. The heavy receipts also
encouraged selling. Atlantic port trances
of wheat and flour were equal to 297.000 hush
els. The domestic visible showed a decrease of
but 80,000 bus nets, Instead of about dOU,WO, as
expected. Covering by shorts at the declines
caused a slight rally near the end and .Ma,
closed at 71 '/jig 72- The weakness of wheat
and liquidation by loading bull concerns weak
ened corn. May closed 4.@Uc lower.
There was an excellent demand for both May
and July oats, the latter option being espe
cially wanted. May closed a shade lower.
Provisions were sustained under the weak
ness of the surrounding grain markets by the
revelations of the monthly stock taking, which
Showed apparently that the great receipts of
hogs are no more thun sufficient to satisfy
current requirements of the foreign and do
mestic consumers. May pork closed 24'Â§7>c
lower and lard and ribs left off practically
unchanged.

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was more variation in local securities
on the morning session, Paauhau Plantation
I advanced to 3!Â»i and Hawaiian Commercial to
| Sslj. Gas and Electric weakened again, 'sell
: ing down to 75%. The dividends of this com
pany will hereafter be at the rate of 5 instead
of D per cent per annum. On the afternoon
board there was a further decline to 74, and a
further advance In Hawaiian to 86.
Mining stocks opened firmer at a slight ad
vance, but the market did not develop the
strength expected to follow the resumption of
pumping." The telegram from the pump was
as follows: "Pump started at 6:86 p. m. Satur
day and has been continuously at work with
out vibration. Water at 8:30 this morning Is
IC6 feet below the 1750-foot level and 133 feet
below the Sutro drain boxes, and Is going down
at the rate of one foot per hour. The water
is going down slowly in the Consolidated Cali
fornia and Virginia 1750-foot level winze, in
the ("hollar combination shaft and In the Hale
and Norcross incline." The feeling was weak
er In the afternoon. . j
A quarterly dividend of 30 cents per share
is now payable by the Stockton Gas and Elec
tric Company.
The usual 60-cent quarterly dividend of th
Market Street Railway will be paid on th
10th.
The Giant Consolidated Powder Company will
! pay a dividend of 60 cents per share on the lOt
The delinquent assessment sale of the Alt
Mining Company will be held to-day. . â 
The second dividend of, the Paauhau Planta
tion Company, of 30 cents per share, will be
paid on the 10th.
The Argonaut mine in Amador County paid
Its regular monthly dividend of $20,000 on the
29th of March.
The official report from the Consolidated Cal
ifornia and Virginia mine Btates that the Corn
stock Pumping Association has been engaged
In the following work at the C. & C. shaft dur
ing the past week: 1400 levelâ The west drift '
has been extended 12 feet; total distance west
from C. & C. shaft, 421 reel. The face is in :
soft hanging wall rock. . 1500 levelâ On this
level a drain was repaired and a tine of four- !
Inch pipe laid to catch up water that was find
ing its way through to the 1650 station. 1650 |
levelâ On this level the water pipe system Is I
being rearranged to guard against fire. 1750
levelâ Work has been continued on repairing !
the winze station and reopening the northwest I
passage through the old stopei between the
1750 and 1650 levels. At present this is the only I
airway between the 1750 and 1650 levels. Neces
sary repairs have been made on the lower
floor below the engine floor leading to the ore
house and waste dumps. The larger part of
the pumping association force being engaged in
placing the rearranged elevator in position, no j
work has been done on the air compressor of
the hoist for the 1750 winze.. All of this work
will be finished before the Fix-Inch air pipe
arrives from the East, which was ordered last
week.
In the Union Consolidated mine west cross
cut No. 1 has been started 100 feet north of
the top of the upraise 2CC feet above the 900
level and advanced 10 feet. The face ' is in
quartz assaying from $1 50 to $2 per ton.
in the Sierra Nevaaa mine the north drift ,
started at a point 187 feet northwest from the
140-foot level station of the shaft, was ad
vanced 8 feet during the week: the total length
251 feet; face in quartz, which assays $1 23 to
$2 per ton. The raise started at a point 223
feet north from the west drift to connect with !
the Rlley tunnel has been advanced 21 feet;
.total length, 37 feet. 900 levelâ south drift
from the too of the raise 80 feet above this
level was advanced 10 feet during: the week;
total length, 132 feet; face in porphyry. .
In the Utah Consolidated mine the north
weet drift, started at a point in main west
drift 120 feet In, was advanced 18 feet in por
phyry; total length, 93 feet.
In the Best & Belcher and Gould & Curry i
mines, on the Sutro tunnel level, they are still
repairing the Osbiston shaft station and are
using aJI good timber in the old sets for these
repairs. The ground is blocky and dangerous.
In the Hale & Norcross mine they are making
good progress in cleaning out and repairing the
connecting drift between the main incline and
the Sutro tunnel south lateral branch.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of exoense.
â¢ Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete seta of charts and sall
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, If any, Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
Lieutenant. V. S. N.. in charge.

the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

CUSTOM HOUSE
Iron Molder Gwynne
Makes a Reform.
HOW HIS DIAMOND WAS LOST
WORTH A HUNDRED, IT WAS AP
PRAISED AT FIFTEEN.
Refused to Accept the Smaller Sum
and the Secretary of the Treas
ury Sees Justice
Done.
John Gwynne, a humble molder at the
â Union Iron Works, has been made, un
wittingly, the instrument for effecting a j
much-needed reform in the San Francis
co Custom-house, namely, that of one
employe â  tiring a receipt from another i
cv passing over any valuable article of j
personal property, such as jewelry and
precious stones. Up to a week or two j
ago these articles passed from one per
son to another in the Custom-house
without a receipt, and in case of loss or j
theft there was no sure way of tracing j
the culprit or lixing the responsibility.
Something more than a year ago John j
Gw.vnne received from his sister in Wales
a diamond ring through the registered
nail. lie had furnished the ring him
eelf, paying $12 for the gold in it, and j
Eent it to his sister, who had the dia
mond set in it as a gift to him.
Oji its arrival at the Tostoffice it was
handed over to a customs officials for ex
amination, the Postoifice authorities tak
ing a receipt for the same. Then it was
tent' to the Appraiser's department, j
where it was appraised by Examiner
George Keynolds at $15, for the purpose
of fixing the duty upon it. When Gwynne j
â  called tor the ring he was informed that
It had been lost in the Custom House. He
was offered $15, the appraised value of
. the piece of jewelry, but refused to ac
cept it, as ho claimed that the ring was I
worth Â£21, or Â»1"5.
Mr. Gwynne, llnding that he could get |
no higher offer, laid the matter before
the Svcrctar.% of the Treasury, and that ;
oihciiil requested Special Agents Smith
and Linck to Investigate. Mr. Gwynne
appeared before them and presented a
ct-rttneate from the English jeweler who
had set the diamond setting forth its
value at JIUI or $H's. Other evidence cor
roborated Mr. Gwynne's statement and i
the .jeweler's certificate, and the result '
was that the special agents made over
tures to Mr. Gwynne for a compromise.
Ih- agreeing to split the difference and
Bi'cept in full payment of his claim $60,
hulf the value of his ring, and let by
gones be bygones. The agents were sat
isfied with the offer and made a report
to the Secretary of the Treasury recom
mending that the San Francisco Custom- i
house be instructed to pay Mr. Gwynne
"*s<;:ln lieu of his ring and take his re
rejpt and a release from all responsibil- \
ity in the matter.
â  VYben the agents were investigating the ,
loss they ino.uired whether there was a :
reason for the absence tn the Custom
house of a system of receipts for portable i
.valuables; such as exists in the registered
mail department of the postoffice. They j
were informed that the receipt system
tvould not be practicable in the Custom
house, but no valid reason was urged for
tills belief. The agent smiled at the ox- ;
cuse and insisted that the system of giv
ing and taking receipts for portable prop
erty should be inaugurated at once. It is
now in full swing and is working like .
plockwork. Henceforth, when an article
:s lost in the Custom-house, the person in i
whose care it disappeared will be known
and the damage can be assessed to the
responsible party.
Itfr. Reynolds' explanation of his ap
praising at $15 a diamond ring containing
U'2 worth of. gold, a setting worth $S ana
a diamond of the first water worth $S3
was that he took the ring to a jeweler,
who informed him that it was worth no j
more than $15. On being asked for the :
name of tho jeweler he replied that he ,
rould not remember.
In their report the agents say that the i
Appraiser's employe honestly believed
the ring to bo worth no more than the j
Value placed by him upon it. he not being
an expert in such matters, and th:!t prob
ably he had not gone to a jeweler at all, j
but thought that he had. the transaction
having taken place considerably more
than a year ago.
DROPPED FROM THE ROLL.
Bix Patrolmen and a Corporal Re
tired by Pension Commissioners.
The Police Pension Commissioners held
their quarterly meeting yesterday morn-
Ing and seven of the men on the list pub
lished exclusively in The Call about three
months ago as slated for retirement had
their names dropped from the roll.
They were Patrolmen Maurice Duane,
\ William McCottrey and Charles A. Hen
nessey for not being: able to do patrol
duty, through continuous sickness, and
William Gaynor, Harrison Mo ran and
Johji Bprminprham and Corporal â¢ â¢ aney
who were retired. Gaynor, Bermingham
Bnd Heaney have served over twenty
years and are over GO years of ago, and
consequently are entitled to a pension;
and Moran. although not exactly serv
ing the full twenty years, will get his
pension, as hi* disability has been caused
by his long devotion to duty.

LOCAL PEDDLERS
OF VICE PLACED
UNDER ARREST
Raid on Phonograph
Parlors.
KANE AND POLICE IN CONCORD
MAYOR PHELAN DENOUNCES A
VILE TRAFFIC.
Authorities Determined to Drive Im
moral Shops Away From tho
Main Thoroughfares
of the City.
The efforts of The Call to secure the
suppression of degrading and indecent
public exhibitions in the phonograph par
lors on the main thoroughfares of the city
are bearing fruit. The police and the of
of the local society for the preven
tion of vice made independent raids yes
terday and the proprietors of three of the
disgusting establishments were placed un
dor arrest. Municipal laws cover the of
fense completely, denning clearly and ex
plicitly what the penalty shall be. The
have in their possession the evl
ili.nce that is necessary to convict. The
offenders have been formally charged
and the affair is now within the jurisdic
tion of the Police Judges.
Vnder these conditions it would seem
that the outcome would be easy to predict.
Experience has taught differently, how
ever, as panderers of filth and corrupters
of children sometimes enjoy political
pulls. Frank Kane, the secretary of the
y for the Prevention of Vice, says
that Police Judges are sometimes very
erratic. An incident that happened yes
terday justified his assertion and explains
his meaning. Justice of the Peace Barry,
whom an accident has raised to the Mig
nity of an acting Police Judge, released
f the offenders on his own recognlz
while Police Judge Graham held an
r for the same offense on a bond of
This fact needs no criticism. In the
same connection it is perhaps slgnflcant
that Police Captain \\ ittm;m did abso
lutely nothing to enforce the law against
;>ublic display of immorality in the
district over which he has supervision,
while Police Captain Spillane acted vlgor
and with success in the raid that is
led to protect decent people and in
nocent children from the contamination of
vice openly flaunted.
Secretary Frank Kane was the first to
yesterday. He secured warrants for
..rsest of M. Kollman of S4S Kearny
street and M. S. Levy at 415 Kearny
street. Tho warrants wore procured on
barge that these men were violating
Order No. - y -~< of the General Orders of
the Board of Supervisors. This order
prohibits the exhibition of Indecent plc
and as Secretary Kane has what
he deems Is sufficient evidence against the
arrested men. ho hopes to secure a con
viction. He has. in his personal investi
gation, seen enough to become an im
portant witness as well as an accuser.
Kollman was taken after his arrest be
fore Acting Police Judge Barry and was
released on his own recognizance. Levy
was liberated upon supplying a bond for
Levy's establishment is one of the
vilest of those that are maintained on the
public thoroughfares. It is located in the
district over which Captain Wlttman ex
es an alleged vigilance. Wittman
has permitted the place to exist for
months in flagrant violation of the law,
and even after his attention had been
i idled to it he saw no reason to take ac
tion.
Acting under orders from Policy Captain
Spillane, Officers Tyrrell and Esola also
made a raid, armed with warrants for
Peter Bacigalupi. the panderer and chief,
and M. Kollman and John Carroll, whose
vice-breeding shop is at 724 Market street.
These three places are the only ones
within the police district governed by Po
ll. â¢ Captain Spillane. But Kollman and
Carroll were arrested, but Bacigalupi
ROl be found. He was reported to
have left the <i sy . but will be arrested as
as he returns.
Tho police are acting In their arrests
upon the authority given in Order LSS7,
in 20, of the General Orders of the
Supervisors. This order is simi
lar In intent to No. 2525. although Secre
tary Kano believes that convictions may
be more easily obtained under No. 2525
than under No. 1557. The examination of
th. arrested men will be held this morn
ing.
It is a matter of Incidental Interest per
haps that Bacigalupi has recently gone
further in his effrontery than ever be
fore. He sought and almost obtained
the right to make his establishment even
more offensive to the public than It is by
displaying a great illuminated sign.
Mayor Phelan and the Supervisors, at the
meeting of the board yesterday, can
; a permit granted to Bacigalupi, at
the last meeting, to erect an advertising
sign in front of his "phonograph par
lors." The matter came up on final pas
sage. The clerk had been reading various
resolutions In a mechanical way, but this
one attracted the attention of his Honor
the Mayor. "Read that again, please,"
he said. When the resolution had been
read he said: "I desire to call the atten
tion of the board to this resolution. It
grants one Bacigalupi, proprietor of a
phonograph and moving-picture show, the
right to erect an attractive sign such as
the theaters have.
"He wants to advertise a lewd picture
show. What Is the pleasure of the board
in this matter?"
Many members objected to tho granting
of the privilege. A motion made by Su-
I ervisor Deasy that the consideration of
the matter be indefinitely postponed was
carried unanimously. The proposed ad
vertisement will not be displayed.
It is not by any means tho intention of
the authorities to stop in the work they
have so well begun. While the arrest
and conviction of the shopkeepers will
do much to cripple the disgusting traf
fic, against which complaint has been
made, something more thorough must be
done before the trade in vice is destroyed.
There is not the slightest doubt that the
vicious machines, the "artoscopes,"
"mutosoopes" and "vitascopes," may be
condemned and confiscated. It is the pur-
POM of the authorities to seize those ma
chines and make their owners fight for
them. In all of the establishments lewd
and ribald songs are ready for the phono
graph and are subject to confiscation.
When tho authorities commence this cru
sade it will be the beginning of the end
of the depraving business.
After the attack hats been completed
upon the source of the evil, raids will be
made upon the saloons and resorts among
which tho vile machines are scattered.
Every week the subjects are changed In
machines and with each succeeding
v,( i k the moving pictures become more
daringly suggestive. The police Intend
to drive these machines out of the sight
of decent men. Above all other things
is it the purpose of the authorities to
dear Market street of its vulgar evils
thai nightly outrage the senses of citi
zens and shock pedestrians with displays
of vice and Immorality.
A Business-Like Burglar.
John Haley, an ex-convict, was ar
rostftl yesterday and booked at the City
Prison on a charge of burglary. On
January 9 he drove up to a grocery at
Sixth and Bryant streets and got the
key to a vacant house at 421 Sixth street,
as he said he was a plumber and had
sent to repair the copper boiler.
He HtDle the boiler and since then the
police had been searching for him. He
made it a practice to drive around with
a horse and wagon and steal anything
he could from vacant houses. Some years
ago he was sent to the State prison
for robbing a man at Tenth and Bryant
streets.

Lillian Post, the actress, was yester
