Agnews by the Insanity Commissioners.
Bhe was taken from the Receiving Hos
pital yesterday afternoon. She had be
come violently Insane.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 4, 1899.
NO MERCHANTS'
COMPROMISE FOR
SCHOOL TEACHERS
They Have a Plan of
Their Own.
BALANCE GOES TO CREDITORS
CURRENT FTTNDS MTJST NOT BE
INTERFERED WITH.
If Tradesmen Do Not Wish to Accept
the Teachers' Terms the Fight
Will Be Inaugu
rated.
The teachers have thrown down the
gauntlet and are out making terms them
selves. They have refused the compromise
submitted by the merchants and have
submitted a plan of their own which, It
has been reported, is acceptable to the
other side; should it not prove so, how
ever, they will fight in court or out of it,
on the balance for the past six months or
on the money for the months to come.
The meeting called for yesterday after
noon attracted several hundred teachers,
the session be-inff in the auditorium of the
Girls' High School. It was for the pur
pose of taking definite action on the ques
tion of compromise, and the action taken
was definite enough for anybody.
A substitute for the plan offered by the
merchants was submitted and was adopt
ed in the form of three resolutions. They
were as follows:
Resolved, That the committee on litigation
be and Is hereby directed to dismiss the
litigation so far as the payment of the so
oalled merchants' claims are concerned, pro
vided :
IâThat1 â That there be no assignment of teachers'
claims.
2â That the holders of the merchants' claims
a^ree not to collect the same out of the moneys
provided for the last six months of the fiscal
year.
3â That If the terms of compromise In ac
cordance with the provisions of this resolu
tion cannot be made, that the committee be
and is hereby directed to prufecute the pend
ing litigation and to take such further legal
steps as may be necessary.
These resolutions were not all adopted
ax the same time. The first two were
brought up by themselves and were
passed and then the last was considered.
It was suggested that instead of settling
the matter right then and there it would
be better to have the committee report
back to the teachers and then allow them
to decide on further proceedings, but it
was finally decided to place the committee
In a position to give the merchants a
definite Idea as to the stand of the teach
ers In the matter.
The meeting opened with an explanation
by F. H. Clark of the Lowell High School
as to matters which had been investigat
ed and Information which had been ob
tained since the previous mooting. It was
shown by him and by subsequent speak
ers that the letters received from the
Auditor. Treasurer and President of the
Board of Kducatlon were written under a
misapprehension. and communications
from the three officials were read to show
the view they had come to take of the
situation. Auditor Wells wrote to say
that he would support the board In its
endeavor to secure to the two coming
months their full share of the half of the
year's appropriation.
The board contended that it was en
titled to one-half of the year's appropria
tion to run the school department from
January to July. I. J. Truman, Treas
urer, wrote to say that when he had ad
vised that the teachers accept the offer of
the merchants he believed It referred to
the $35,000 balance from the last half of
the fiscal year. Instead of the funds for
the current half as well. He declared
that under such circumstances the teach
ers should never compromise. President
Bergerot wrote that he, too, had misun
derstood the terms of the compromise,
and so he would withdraw his recom
mendation that they accept, in view of
the increased importance which tho mnt
ter had takt-n In his eyes, and ho would
not take the responsibility of advising
thorn.
A resolution was adopted requiring tho
litigation committee to mak? a report
upon its finances and also if there Is any
thing due. The resolution at the same
time pledged the teachers to pay prompt
ly all that may be owing.
"On motion It was decided to fill tho va
cancy on the litigation committee made
by the resignation of Professor Lyser,
and Mr. Clark was proposed. l T ndor the
formation of the committee, however, the
only one eligible was a grammar school
teacher, and Mr. Clark was of the High
School. Mrs. L. K. Burke, principal of
the Columbia School, was therefore added
to the committee.
looses Her Position.
Judge Hunt denied a motion for a new
trial in the ease of Elizabeth P. Bradley
against the Hoard of Education yester
day. Miss Bradley sought to be rein
stated in the School Department as a
special teacher of drawing, but the court
refused to reinstate her. holding that the
School Board had authority to abolish
all special classes.
PICNICKERS IN COURT.
Aftermath of the Opening Day of the
Season at Schuetzen.
Several participants in the opening pic
nic of the season at Schuetzen Park Sun
day made their appearance in the Police
Courts yesterday, but the Judges were
lenient with them and they were dis
charged.
Richard Eagan and James Murphy, two
husky youths, quarreled about the tem
porary companionship of a young girl at
the picnic, but they ciifi not come to blows
till they reached Third and Mission streets
on their way home. Murphy was getting
the worst of the fight when Policeman
Menlhan happened along and placed both
under arrest for disturbing the peace. In
Judge Conlan's court yesterday morning
they refused to divulge the name of the
young girl, and as they shook hands and
agreed to be friends the Judge dismissed
the case.
A. S. Field, a painter from Stockton,
was also at the picnic with Emma Hold
and Nellie Doherty. It was proved by the
two women that Field had thrown a $10
gold piece on the counter at the park for
drinks and the bartender nearly fainted at
the flight of so much coin. The Judge
thought it was lucky for Field that he got
back to the city In safety after recklessly
displaying so much wealth.
The charge against Field was for steal
ing $24 from Emma Meyer, 335 Bush
street. He had called upon Miss M?yer
Sunday morning and she rushed the
"growler." After Field departed she al
leges that she missed the $24 and sus
pected him of taking It. When she learned
that Field had displayed a $10 gold piece
at the picnic her suspicions were con
firmed and she had him arrested. Field
declared that the $10 piece was his own
property, and the Judge gave him the
benefit of the doubt and dismissed the
case.
Struck by a Car.
James Burns, the driver of a dirt cart,
was seriously injured last evening. Burns
was driving along Folsom street and
when near Sixth his cart was run into
by an electric car. Burns was thrown
from his spat, sustaining a laceration of
the forehend and a deep cut over the left
eye. One of his ribs was also fractured.
He was taken to the Receiving Hospital,
where his Injuries were dressed by Po
lice Surgeon Well.
Will Marry To-Day.
Frederick A. Driscoll and Katheryn M.
Pouleon will be married to-day. Driscoll
is a prominent young druggist of this city,
but for some reason has endeavored to
keep his coming marriage secret. The li
cense was obtained on the Ist Inst. and to
day will see the ceremony performed.

VARIETY AND
OPERA AGAIN
RULE THE WEEK
Success Awaits the
Tivoli's Spectacle.
CUT RATES AT THE GRAND
BOSTONIANS HAPPILY REVIVE
"THE SERENADE."
Wilder, Kara and Josephine Gassman
Score Big Hits in a Splendid
Programme at the
Orpheum.
The Tivoli's after-Easter extrava
ganza, "Beautiful Goldenlocks," which I
is written and borrowed by George
B. Lask, with music from every- |
where and everybody, was given out j
last night to a fair-sized audience, j
The first night of a new piece at the
Tivoli is generally subject to halts and
hitches and this one was no exception.
The Tivoli's "Beautiful Goldenlocks" {
will have to be trimmed and brushed
down. This can be accomplished in a
night or two; and then, by all the signs
of previous Eddy-street successes, the
production ought to be good for quite
a run. For it is stuffed with just the
sort of tunes, pictures and nonsense
that the patrons like.
Scenically it is perhaps the neatest
achievement in the history of the house.
The rose-bower scene in the second act
is as gorgeous as the interior of a ten
dollar Easter egg, and the scene of the
Prince's palace is as incandescent as
old David Henderson at his best. In
the rose scene fifteen girls dance a cozy
little ballet, full of pretty figures, and j
a soloist foots it daintily over a skip- j
ping rope. In the same scene Ed i
Stevens sings another song of many I
nations, while the girls contribute a j
complete lightning change of costume
to each chorus in full view of the au
dience â an odd and picturesque idea
cleverly worked out. By the time the ;
fourth chorus was reached last night
the Tivuladies were down to close j
tunics and knee-breechea. The crowd I
encored wildly, but the raiment re- !
mained stationary.
In the palace scene in the last act
Mr. Stevens gives an exhibition in leg
erdemain. He started out by asking
any person in the audience to please
give him n small oblong table thirty- I
two inches in height, and a bunch of :
canary birds. And would any clergy- j
man present kindly oblige him with a j
pack of cards. And everybody thought
that the Stevens necromancy was go
ing to be merely another of the Stevens
jokes. But it turned out quite another j
thing. The comedian displayed a re- j
markable fluency in the sleight-of-hand
tricks (and the; are always the best) ; !
and a fair manipulation of the ma- ,
gician's apparatus. These apparatus ;
tricks will doubtless improve with a ,
few performances, and the sleieht-of
hand will be even better for a more ! '
constant flow of side talk. Silence is ! i
the modern magician's worst enemy.
All in all, the show is jolly, fresh and
entertaining in the right irresponsible
way. What little plot there is is based
on the tales of "Beauty and the Beast." j
and "Cinderella," with generous inter- |
ruptions by dances, songs and special- I (
ties. Miss Lachter walks and warbles \
through the title part; Miss Holmes i
and Miss Hill are courageously the |
ugly sisters; Miss Helen Merrill is the j
shapely good fairy in tropic clothes,
and Miss Meyers is th. Bowery boy. as
bad as only a woman can make him. i
Mr. Stevens is programmed as Mr. All
sortsofvice, a modern Mephisto, and \
the part is elastic enough to give him >
plenty of good comic play. Branson is ;
something bettor than his usual self
as the father of the girls, and Messrs.
Pruette and Schuster are as ever. Mr.
Qerorne, the tenor, Is the Prince trans
formed into a bear. With the furs on
he does not sing much. I enjoy him
served that way.

Grand Opera-House.
Mr. Morosco has partially accepted the
suggestion offered in Sunday's Call by
setting a number of orchestra seats aside
at 50 cents and 75 cents and reducing the
entire orchestra to 50 cents. It is to be
hoped that this will attract the patronage
the Southwell company deserves. The ex- i
cellent production of "The Black Hussar'
is on for all of this week, giving way to
"The Queen's Lace Handkerchief." At
to-night's performance Miss Eleanor
Guistl will sing Nina, and Daisie Thome
Rosetta. Mira Delamotta, the other tenor, ,
will sing the title part.
Orpheum.
Josephine Gassman, a San Francisco
girl, aided and abetted by a brace of wee
pickaninnies as black as Miss Gassman '
makes up, divides the honors at the Or- :
pheum this week with Kara, who plays
toss-up with a table, a hatrack and an
able-bodied man simultaneously and does
other wonderful feats in the juggling line.
Two years ago Miss Gassman began her ,
professional career as a soubrette at a
local theater. Now she is doing the clev
erest "coon" turn yet seen here. Her ,
two miniature assistants are a clever at
traction in themselves.
Kara is billed as the "world's greatest
juggler," and there doesn't seem to be
anybody in these parts who cares to dis
pute his claim. The Hengler sisters are
bewitching dancers, but were they to for
get to vocalize nobody In the audience
would call their attention to the over
sight.
Marshall P. Wilder is artistically enter
taining to the last degree, while Polk (?)
and Kolllns, ban joists, the last of the ,
new faces, play everything from ragtime
to classics.
Of the holdovers, the Pantzer brothers
continue to delight and astonish the pat
rons with their finished act, while the
Matweef troupe are as popular as they
were last week. James H. Manning and
Willie Weston repeat their musical turn, ,
"The Irish Pawnbroker," while the Or
pheum orchestra continues to grind out
the old familiar marches and two-steps
between acts that everybody has ample
opportunity to become thoroughly famil
iar with.
. Alhambra.
Agnes Herndon commenced the third
week of her engagement at the Alhambra
last night in an English drama called
"Married, Not Mated." The play Is by
far the best the present management has
presented. It served to interest the audi
ence and was well acted. Miss Herndon
gave a clever performance of Lady Vida
Harrington, and a really artistic portrai
ture of Susan Scroope was given by
Laura Alberta, who scored an unmistaka
ble hit. "Â«_
Alcazar.
The presentation of Belaaco and De
Mine's "Charity Ball" filled the cozy au
ditorium of the Alcazar last night with
an audience that laughed and cried by
turns. It is a play that affords opportu
nity for every member of a large com
pany to do artistic work, and' the excel
lent company at the Alcazar embraced
and one another. Ernest Hastings as
John Van Buren, Adelaide Fitz Allen as
Ann Kruger, Margaret Marshall as Mrs.
Van Buren, Juliet Crosby as Phyllis Lee,
Laura Crews as Bess, Clarence Montaine
as Judge Knox and Daniel Halifax as
Alec caught the sympathies and drew
smiles and .tears from the appreciative
audience.

The Chutes.
Joe Storms Jr., orang-outang, Sally,

the latest additions to the vaudeville
rankâ As a "triumvirate" they made
their appearance at the Chutes Free j
Theater last evening and stood the blaze
of the calcium fairly well. Joe donned
his whiskers, tipped his hat on the side:
and began to smoke his pipe when the
band played "'St. Patrick's Day." Then
he became embarrassed. "Sally in Our
Alley" was substituted and the â  chim

Said Chicken?" and the accompanying
lecture on the Darwinian theory was

YEARS OF TORTURE.
Relief at Hand Had He Only Known Where
to Look For It.
Let us, my good friends, always suit
our tools to the work to be done. We
do not trim our nails with broadswords
or carve statues with adzes. Now a
word is a tool just as a hoe is, or a ra
zor. The figure of Liberty on a quarter
of a dollar is impressed there by a ma
chine made for the purpose. By the
same token words were invented to im
print thoughts and ideas on the minds
of others. And every word or expres
sion has a currency meaning of its own
so long as we refrain from using it
in a slack or careless way.
Just as the Lord Mayor's coach, or
one's own best clothes are not intended
for every little trumpery occasion, so
great words should be reserved till the
time comes when nothing else will do.
When a man singes his whiskers by the
slovenly handling of a lighted match
we do not rouse up the fire department.
Now the word "excruciating" is one
of the strongest words in the English
language. It tingles with sharp signifi
cance as a black cat's back snaps with
electricity on a frosty night. Only shal
low people will take liberties with this
word. In our ordinary talk it does not
come properly into play once a year;
and rightly so. For it alludes to a kind
of mental or physical suffering that we
desire to avoid thinking or speaking
of. If the root meaning of the word
"excruciating" escapes you for a mo
ment look it up in the dictionary. It is
a word with a pedigree, and its ances
tors have supped with horrors since
men began to make history.
The question then that you and I
have to settle is, whether Mr. Geo. B.
Simpson employed this dignified and
exclusive word justifiably when he said,
"I was troubled twelve years with
much pain in the small of my back. So
continuous was it that I ceased to take
pleasure in work. Indeed I ceased to
take pleasure altogether. With me life
got to be a thing to endure, not to en
joy. I am speaking within bounds
when I say that at times these pains
were excruciating.
"With the pains in my back I suf
fered from terrible periodic headaches
and profound mental depression. I was
a prey to anxiety and apprehension, the
cause of which I was unable to lo
ratp or understand. Yet to look at me
you would hardly have fancied my true
condition. Sometimes when appearing
at my best and receiving the congratu
lations of my friends on what they were
pleased to call my obvious improve
ment, I still felt wretched and miser
able. But nobody sympathized with
me. I suppose it was because I was not
emaciated or run down as persons with
dangerous diseases are commonly sup
posed to be.
"While on this point, I may perhaps
be permitted to say that affections of
the kidneys, even Bright's disease
itself, often co-exist with a generally
healthy external appearance on the
part of the patient. The poor fellow
may be within a few weeks of his death
and yet to the uninstructed eye look
well enough to pass for a sound man.
"The secretion from my kidneys was

high colored and offensive, with a con
siderable brick dust deposit. I can
not set forth in writing what I suffered',
and shall not try. The headaches alone
I thought a sufficient punishment for
such unconscious offenses in the matter
of eating and drinking as had brought
this complaint upon me."
[The editor begs to interrupt Mr.
Simpson to remind him, and others as
well, that in inflicting penalties Nature
never stops for a moment to consider
whether the offenses were consciously
committed or not. Juries and judges
are continually moved either by sym
pathy for the prisoner or prejudice
against him. Nature is cold as a tomb
stone, yet perfectly fair and impartial.
Whether you take a dose of poison ig-,
norantly or unintentionally, or with the
purpose to commit suicide, the result is
j precisely the same. Nature will not
push you over the cliff, neither will she
put out her hand to save you from
being dashed to pieces. Think of this
when you are tempted to meddle with
her arrangements.]
"The doctors," continues Mr. Simp
son, "gave me no relief, although I con
sulted many and followed their direc
tions faithfully. I do not blame them,
for they did their best; and I cannot
praise them as their best was no ad
vantage to me.
"It was my custom to buy my toilet
articles at the Walker-Rintels Drug
Co., 244 Boylston street, and one day I
told Mr. D. Wallace Rintels about my
condition and the failure of the doctors
to help me. He at once suggested the
use of Warner's Safe Cure. As to the
! virtue of this medicine, although I ha
â¢ hfard of it, I was very skeptical. I
| considered the advertised statements
! of its merits exaggerated, yet I bought
a bottle on the bare chance of its doing
me good. After having taken a few
doses my headaches were certainly less
frequent and less intense. Greatly de
\ lighted at this hopeful outcome I kept
on taking the medicine regularly and
improved steadily; and before long the
symptoms I have named wholly disap
| peared.
"At the date of this writing I have
j been well long enough to make sure
that my cure is radical and permanent.
In spite of the pain and peril of those
twelve fearful years I have to-day as
good an appetite, as clear a head and
as sound a body as any man in Boston,
As an illustration of it I need only say
that the very Insurance company which
had previously rejected me on account
of an 'Incurable disease of the kidneys'
has since written a heavy policy on my
life. I weigh more than I used to do
and my flesh is healthy and solid. I am
rid of the puffiness which marks the
chronic sufferer from kidney trouble.
And for this transformation I hereby
acknowledge Indebtedness to Warner's
l Safe Cure and that only." George B.
Simpson, 23 Church street, Boston.
What is popularly called kidney
trouble is shown by modern investiga
tion to have several forms. It is al
ways a dangerous and deceptive condi
tion, sometimes slowly and sometimes
rapidly progressive, and liable to term
inate fatally even when the immediate
symptoms seem to be least alarming.
Thousands of casualties attributed to
other causes are really due to diseased
kidneys. The success for many years
of Warner's Safe Cure in relieving and
curing this subtle disease has won for
it the world-wide reputation it now en-
I joys. When kidney symptoms declare
| themselves, do not hesitate to use War
ner's Safe Cure. The risk is not in re
sorting to this remedy, but in neglect
' ing it.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
SHALL FABIOLA
RAVE A RACE
TRACK BENEFIT?
T. H. Williams Has
Made the Offer.
QUESTION UNDER DEBATE
DAN BURNS' POLITICS IS A BIG
FACTOR.
Acquisition of Funds Said to Be an
Urgent Necessity for the Treas
ury of the Great Insti
tution.
â â â^â _
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, I
90S Broadway, April 3.
Snail Fabiola have for its annual benefit |
a May day fete, an equine parade, a bene- |
fit at the race track, or nothing? This is
the question with which the directors of
the hospital find themselves face to face.
For four years past the greatest social
event of the year has been Fabiola day.
May day is less than a month off, and as
yet no notice has been given of what may
be expected.
Last year the racetrack management
gave Fabiola the entire proceeds of one
day at the track, and hung up a splendid
purse for a race known as the Fabiola
derby. On this occasion T. H. Williams
and his friends forbade any one from en
tering the track without paying their dol
lar. He was so interested in Fabiola that
he personally declined the offer of a pass
to enter his own property, and on the i
eventful day Mr. Williams and the officers
of the track passed through the gates,
accidentally, of course, just at the mo
ment when several of the lady directors
of Fabiola were entering. The ladies were
more than gratified to see Mr. Williams
and his friends contribute their dollars
and attached the tickets to their coat j
buttons with as much humility as the j
humblest tout about the place.
As a result of Fabiola day at the race
track a sum exceeding- $4000 was netted
for the hospital. There was, however, I
Borne opposition to the benefit. Many min
isters denounced the scheme from their
pulpits as being unworthy of such a no
lle institution. This charge was ably
defended by the ladies, who declared that
they were working for the most unself
ish of motives and were giving all their
time and energy toward building up thai
benevolent Institution, and were, there
fore, entitled to immunity from criticism.
One of the lady managers put forward
the argument that there is somewhere in j
the Scriptures a story telling how the!
Nazarene advocated even the breaking j
of the Sabbath to pull a sheep out of the I
pit. because it was a deed of charity. The :
deduction from this was very simple, and
In spite of the opposition the ladies went
ahead.
A few days ago Thomas H. Williams
In a lettrr very cordially reminded the
ladies that May day was again approach
ing and tendered them the use of the
track on the same conditions as last year. '
The letter was discussed at a meeting of
the lady managers, but as it was de- !
sired to olrtain the expression of the full i
board before taking decisive action the
matter was laid over until the next meet
ing, which occurs next Tuesday.
Many of the ladies are of the same opin- !
ion as they were last year and believed !
that In this case the end justifies the I
means, while others doubt the propriety
of accepting a benefit from the track
managers.
The recent bitter fight over Colonel
Burns In the Legislature and the bitter
ness with which Burns and his gambling
fraternity were denounced by the Minis
ters' Association of Oakland are not for
gotten, and the action the ministers
â would take should a benefit be accepted
from the racetrack management is hard
ly problematical. Last year, when the
opposition was engendered, very little was
known generally of the connection of Col
onel Burns with the Oakland track, and I
the ministers were more general than i
specific In their denunciation. This year
the associations of Burns and his outfit
are prominent in the minds of everybody
especially in this county, and one of the
ministers, who would not assume to speak
â with authority for his brethren, said to
day that there Is no doubt that their opin
ion of the racetrack benefits Is the same
as It was last year.
Fabiola. like all other institutions of
Its character, has suffered financially be
cause of the general closeness of money
during the past few years, and it needs
all the help it can command. It would
be a great hardship for it to be deprived
of four or five thousand dollars at this
time, according to the managers, who
deeply feel the responsibility of their
trust.
The question of the Fabiola benefit Is
the most prominent of the hour In Oak
land, for It Interests the management so
ciety at large, the church folks and the
racetrack management, all to a great de
gree. :

Home was destroyed by fire, and for a

I There is some doubt as to whether the law goes into effect to-day or to
Â£ morrow, but Senator Dargie is too good a citizen and has too conscientious

/ signed by their writers.
y This practical demonstration of the absurdity of the new law is all that
0, is needed to convince the public of its foolishness. One of the articles

*. lows only because there was a reasonable doubt ac to his intent to beat the

X says: "For once a murderer will secure his deserts upon whom will not be
hnished the sympathy of foolish women and weak-minded sentimentalists,
y for it is Impossible for any one to entertain the slightest feeling of pity
Z for a man who could flog his child to death."
Last night another child-beater was lodged in the County Jail, and in
*, presenting the news Senator Dargie's paper tells the truth regarding him.
t This article under the law requires a signature and consequently is duly
5 attested, although the girl's body is covered with black and blue marks
X which the prisoner admitted Inflicting upon her for taking a piece of
Â£ Easter cake.
% Without the signature any "grafter" could ask the courts to compel the
publisher of the Tribune to pay him $2000 for these articles.

Fractured His Skull.
OAKLAND, April 3.â John Broderiok.
residing at 813 Filbert street, was found
lying on the sidewalk on Linden street,
near Seventh street, this morning with
his skull fractured. He was taken to the
Receiving Hospital, where, after being
somewhat revived, he stated that he had
i been knocked down and kicked in the
j head by Frank Jackson, a colored man.
Jackson was soon rounded up by De
tectives Shorey and Hodgkins and is be-
I ing detained at the City Prison, awaiting
I developments In his victim's condition.
The physicians at the hospital this
afternoon trephined Broderick's skull and
his condition is serious.
Four Divorces Granted.
OAKLAND, April 3.â Superior Judge
I Ellsworth to-day granted four decrees of
i divorce. Mary A. Case was given a
i divorce from James K. Case on the
i grounds of extreme cruelty and willful
' j desertion. She was given the custody of
! the minor child and $25 monthly alimony.
i Maud Dawson sustained her charge of
1 willful desertion against William W.
â  Dawson and was also granted a divorce.
Mary E. Bornhorst was given a legal
separation from Herman Bornhorst on
the ground of extreme cruelty and was
allowed to resume her maiden name, that
of Mary E. Wetter. Mary E. Noble was

WILLIAM WIEGMAN
SAYS HE WAS BUNKOED
NOW SEEKS TO REGAIN HIS LOST
PROPERTY.
Claims He Signed a Bill of Sale While
Afflicted With a Tem
porary Mania.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, April 3.
William Wiegman filed a suit in the
Superior Court this afternoon against
George E. Beehe and Fred Wiegman, the
latter his cousin, alleging that he was de
frauded out of his interest in a thriving
general merchandise business at Al
varado.
According to his complaint he entered
into a copartnership with Beebe on Jan
uary 2. 1897, Each partner put Into the
business $2500 and the partnership was to
extend over five years. On January 30
of this year Beebe borrowed from Fred
Wiegman $1000, giving him a promissory
note, payable in one year. This money
was also invested In the business. On
February 20 the business was prosperous
and the firm's indebtedness did not ex
ceed JCSO, except the note mentioned,
which plaintiff had also signed, but the
store, stock, fixtures and good will of the
business at this time was worth $SOOO.
About this time, the complaint all* p^s.
plaintiff became very sick, laboring under
temporary mania, and was wholly un
able raid Incompetent to transact any
business, and while in this condition the
.;ints willfully, knowingly and
fraudulently persuaded plaintiff by
threats, menace and force to sign and
execute a certain paper, the purport of
which he did not then understand, but is
now Informed is a bill of sale of all his
rights and interest In the business and as
sets of the firm of Weigman & Beebe to
defendant, his cousin, Mr. Wiegman.
Plaintiff in conclusion alleges that no
consideration was ever paid or agreed
upon, and on February 27 the entire, busi
ness was Bold by defendants to one Har
vey.
Wherefore Mrs. Wiegman asks $10,000
damages on the ground that his business
has been destroyed and his credit and
reputation seriously impaired; besides
$500u for punitive damages for the fraud.
Only last week William Wiegman
Fought to have a receiver appointed while
his interests might be determined legally,
but Judge Ogden on that occasion held it
inadvisable to interfere, since he consid
ered Harvey a purchaser in good faith.
It developed at the hearing of Wlegf
man's petition at that time that his sick
liess was due to too free an access to the
flowing bowl, which rendered him incom
petent to transact business, and for which
hi v, as under treatment, and wherefore he
had sold out.
The trial of the present suit promises to
be sensational.
CHRISTIAN ' GOVERNMENT.
OAKLAND, April 3â A national re
form convention for the purpose of con
sidering the Christian principles of civil
government held in the lecture room of
the First Presbyterian Church to-night
was well attended. Rev. J. M. French
presided and spoke on the essential ele
ments of Christian citizenship and the
advisability of introducing into the pub
lic schools the Bible as an auxiliary text
book. He was followed by Rev. Dr. H.
H. George, who discoursed on the sub
ject of "Christian as Against Secular
Government."
Rev. W. H. Fulton. Rev. Elmer Me-
Burney, Rev. Mr. Jones, Rev. Mr. Ellis
and J. T. Robinson were appointed a
committee to draft resolutions voicing
the sentiments of the convention, to be
reported at to-morrow night's session, at
which time the temperance and Philip
pine problems will be discussed by Mrs.
R. U. Johnson and Rev. E. R. Bennett.
There will also be a session to-morrow
afternoon, when the speakers will be
Rev. Floyd J. Mynard, Mrs. H. H
George and Rev. E. R. Dille. In the
evening the convention concludes.

Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 3.
The new City Council assumed charge
of the city's affairs to-night. The fea
ture of the meeting was the reading of a
message from Mayor Snow. It was a
pertinent document, words being neither
minced nor wasted.
"We find the city in a pit," says the
Mayor. "We shall hurt somebody getting
it out, and probably do ourselves some
injury; but the peculiar situation is both
the reason for our action and our excuse.
1 recommend that the salary of every of
ficer and employe that is not fixed by the
charter be reduced until June 30 aboift 25
per cent.
"Every incoming Mayor has read an in
augural message; the desk has filed li
away and it has been forgotten within
ten days. You will notice that I have
adopted a different plan. In my communi
cation I have made nine suggestions and
the same number of ordinances and reso
lutions are respectfully presented here
with for such action as your wisdom di
rects.
"To meet the deficit of $60,000, the Coun
cil will probably reduce every salary now
fixed by resolution or ordinance 23 peh
cent. Policemen will probably be retired
from the positions of captain and detec
tives for the purpose of saving their in
creased salaries. The expense of house
numbering will be discontinued. One
half of the street lights are to be shut
off; one-half the street sweeping to be
discontinued and also one-half of the
â  sprinkling. The bond Interest
money due on coupons July 2 will be used
for general expenses; and after all, the
statutory salaries, amounting to $27,000 for
the months of April, May and June, will
have to be carried over into the next tin
cal year.
"It was known when the last tax levy
was made that to do what was then al
proposed would require a levy of
$1 \Ci Without making any change in the
Erogramme of expenses the levy was
\. .1 at $121; deficit of $60,000 Is the re-
"The legal limit of taxation by the city
is $1, ex( ept for bonds and interest. The
assessment mil dropped last year $6,000,
--000 iiinl Is likely to be reduced this year
12 OCmi 000 more. The property of the
whole city Is likely to be valued by the
''ounty Assessor this year for only $40,
--000 000. This valuation at the full l<>gal
rate of taxation will furnish only $400,000.
The additional income from other
sources is not likely to be more than
$350,000, or $750,000 all told.
"The cost for maintenance alone for
the year closing June, 1898, was over $751,
--(kii) The cost of street lighting In IbSJ
was $36,000. It is now $72,000. The cost
of our schools in 1890 was $200,000. It is
now $300,000. In 1890 only $55,000 was ex
pended upon our streets. Last year $80,
--000 was expended for like purposes. Our
Police Department expense has grown
from $48,000 in 1MÂ» to require $78,000 for
maintenance. And our Fire Department
that cost in IS9O $56,000 for maintenance
now costs $82,000. The reduction of
$8 000 000 In the assessment roll since 1897
reduces the Income of the city $80,000 per
year upon a tax rate of $1. The cost of
the city government must be reduced the
coming liscal year $w,OOO or the present
condition of affairs will be repeated one
year from to-day."
To the School Directors Mayor Snow
sent the following additional suggestion:
"1 have one other matter that I desire
to speak of. It is very probable that
some attempt may be made during the
year to amend the city charter. The
present charter attempts to require the
Board of l'ublic Works to act as your
executive arm, while the general law
seems to ret this provision aside. When,
we come to amend the charter It will be
necessary to know for a fact whether
your honorable body controls a school
district and Is subject to the general
laws governing such districts or subject
to charter provisions. I desire to request
that you join if necessary in a friendly
suit that may be hereafter arranged to
determine the legal relation of your hon
orable board to the city of Oakland."
When the Council met Councilman H.
D. Rowe of the Seventh Ward was unani
mously elected president. He waa an ac
tive member of the last Council.
Those who expected surprises at the
Council meeting were not disappointed,
for they came in bunches. Mayor Snow
read his own message and handed In the
ordinances and resolutions necessary to
make it effective.
Councilman (Jirard made the first attack
on gambling by calling up the ordinance
prohibiting keno, which was held up by
the old License Committee. Mr. Glrard
made a powciful plea for the ordinance.
"I hav*e made inquiry," he said "and know
whereof I speak. There are in the heart
of this city twelve or llfteen hells where
our youth are being corrupted and made
dishonest. At this present moment from
500 to 1000 people are gambli^; and ap
purentlv there Is no ordim^T-e to stop
thÂ»m. In this number are clerks and stu
dents of our high school and university,
young men who cannot do more than sup
port themselves, and they are sitting
around risking 1 their money and perhaps
that of their employers at keno. These
bloodsuckers who run these games should
be stopped at once. I know Just where
they are located, and although there are
signs up, 'No boys under 21 admitted,'
that is the most deceptive of subterfuges,
for I have seen in one saloon as many as
eight boys under 16 years old.
If this ordinance will not stand the test
of the courts, then we will pass one im
posing a license of $1000 a night on these
bloodsuckers, and no one can stop us do-
Ing that." As Mr. Girard is chairman of
the License Committee he commands
much authority. The ordinance was unan
imously passed to print.
Another ordinance, introduced by Mayor
Snow, was read and sent to committee. It
prohibits gambling of all kinds, and also
makes it a misdemeanor to lease a place
for gambling.
Measures were also introduced abolish
ing the office of one police captain and of
two detectives; also arranging for doing
away with one-half of the street sweep
ing, sprinkling and street lighting for the
remaining three months of this year;
also for reducing every salary fixed by
the Council and for repealing the ordi
nance appropriating $60,000 for building a
storm sewer In Cemetery Creek. All
these measures will be decided by the
committee of the whole, which will meet
with the Board of Works on Wednesday
evening.
City Clerk Tompklns was reappointed
and he at once named Ed Holand, the
incumbent, as his deputy.
Fred Murdock will cease to be City
Wharfinger on April 30 and Charles W.
Agner was appointed his successor.
Henry Powers will cease to be Pound
master on April 10 and Herbert Swift will
be his successor. John Wilds was ap
pointed to enter upon his twenty-first
year as head janitor of the City Hall. A
resolution was Introduced declaring the
place of Night Janitor Tim Holland va
cant and naming Frank Colver as his
successor, but the matter was sent to
committee.

SHE FEARS VISITS
OF HER EX-HUSBAND
E. A. YON SCHMIDT WANTS TO
SEE HIS CHILDREN.
His Ex-Wife Produces Letters He
Has Written Since the Legal
Separation.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 3.
Captain E. A. yon Schmidt and his di
vorced wife, Isabella yon Schmidt, ap
peared before Judge Greene to-day and
created a lively scene. The well-known
yachtsman, through his attorney, Hugh
Aldrich, moved the court to modify the
divorce decree recently granted so that
ho might be permitted to occasionally
visit his three children at their Alameda
home. Judge Greene, however, "sat
down" upon the captain pretty hard.
"It's $100 to nothing," said his Honor,
"that If you go to the home of this
woman you would certainly commit some
breach of propriety. No, no, you cannot
go there."
Isabella yon Schmidt held the same
opinion. She was fortified with several
letters she had recently received from
Captain yon Schmidt, wherein he b<
for permission to call at her home and
see the children. In one of these letters
he accused her of having perjured herself
at the divorce trial. Again, while Cap
tain Yon Schmidt was on the witness
stand he tried to convince the court that
he could call at his former wife's home
without committing any breach of pro
priety. Judge Greene, however, decided
that the captain should be allowed to see
his children at some friend's home twice
each month.

Huber's Death Confirmed.
OAKLAND, April 3.â There is now no
doubt that Henry Huber, the Oakland
boy who was with the troops at Manila,
was killed by the Filipinos. Additional
Information has been received in this city
substantiating the story published In The
Call a week ago. The lad's parents have
no longer any hope that their son is still
alive.

UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA NEWS
BERKELEY, April 3.â A determined ef
fort is to be made to arouse the same col
lege spirit and student enthusiasm over
the approaching baseball contest with
Stanford as marked the great football
Beason of last year. The college men have
begun to seÂ« that their baseball players
have, the same support and encourage
ment that helped their gridiron veterans
to the victory of last Thanksgiv4ng. The
same spirit which manifested itself there
is beginning to materialize once more. It
broke out for the first time this afternoon.
Placards announcing a grand baseball
rally had been posted in conspicuous
places in the campus during the day. The
notices brought a throng of students down
to the bleachers on the lower campus
shortly before 4 o'clock. The old-time
feeling was abroad and it showed itself in
the old ways. As Coafh Corbett came on
to the diamond with his men and began
arranging their positions, those on the
bleachers remembered Cochran and hin
f;iants, and the California yell went ring
ng over the field. It was the first time
that the baseball men have been really
cheered this season, but the spell was
broken. Again and again the yell was
given and it came out at intervals during
the practice.
It had the desired effect upon the men
and they put in the snappiest practice of
this term. The same thing will be car
ried out dally, from now on, until the
Blue and Gold faces the Cardinal. Mean
while the enthusiasts are organizing a
rooter's club, similar to that of last term.
Each member has to come to the games
decked in a blue and gold hat. The club
will form the yelling center of the Cali
fornia section at the big games, and will
enliven the proceedings with baaeball
songs and joshes.
The project of a University circus for the
benefit of the Athtletic Association is
gaining favor. It has been found possible
to hold the affair within the enclosure
around the cinder track. The promoters
of the plan have estimated that it will
cost something like $300, and they will lay
this report before the next meeting of
the Athtletic Association. Should it re
colve the sanction of that body the circus
will be held toward the close of the pres
ent term.

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for furnishing all the furniture,
fixtures, finishes, etc., for the Hall of Justice
and Morgue buildings.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of Super-
visors of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, April 4, 1899.
In accordance with resolution No. 25X7
(Fourth Series) of the Hoard of Supervisors,
Fealed proposals will be received in open ses-
sion of the Board of Supervisors on MON-
DAY AFTERNOON, April 17, 1599, from 2:30
to 3 o'clock, for furnishing all the furniture,
fixtures, finishes, etc., fur the Hall of Justice
and Morgue buildings, in strict accordance
with the plans and specifications prepared by
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, and filed In
the office of the clerk of the Board of Super-
visors on April 3, 1899, copies of which can
ba seen by Intending bidders at office of
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, at No. 26
Montgomery street.
Bidden will bid on this work with the un-
derstanding that nil the material mentioned
in the specifications/shall be manufactured In
the State of 'â¢â â ilifrfrnia. and that all labor
necessary In the manufacture of said mate-
rial and all labor necessary in the erection
and completion of the work mentioned In the
specifications shall be performed by parties
residing in the State of California.
And It Is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
persons employed on the above work, and
that the minimum rate of wages to be paid
shall be two ($2) dollars per day.
Bidders will also be required to Biibmit with
their bids a certified check on some bank in
the city and county of San Francisco in a
sum equal to one-tenth (1-10) of the amount
of their bid, and the party or parties to
â whom the contract or contracts may be
awarded will be required to enter Into a bond
In a cum equal to one-fourth ('4) of the
amount of their contract, signed by two or
more good and sufficient sureties, to be ap-
proved by the Board of Supervisors, for the
faithful performance of tne contract.
Bidders will estimate and state a price on
the following subdivisions of the material to
be supplied and work performed, to wit:
Firstâ For all furniture, including all metal
grill work In connection with same; metal
railings for prisoners' docks; all courtroom
and other railings; hardware; metal shelving
and vault doors; ventilators, Bertillon sys-
tem- photographic appliances and kitchen fur-
niture.
Secondâ For gas and electric combination
fixtures.
Thirdâ For water filtering system.
Fourthâ For tinting and decorating.
Fifthâ For electrical apparatus, switch
board and telephone Bystem.
Bidders will bid with the distinct under-
standing that separate contracts will he
awarded for each class to the lowest respon-
sible bidder.
For any further information required as to
receiving copies of plans and specifications
bidders will apply to the architects, Messrs.
Shea & Shea.
The party or parties to whom each one of
the contracts may be awarded will be re-
quired, prior to or at the time of the execn-
tlon of said contracts, to pay one-fifth the
cost of advertising this and a former notice
in three dally newspapers, and each success-
ful bidder to also pay the cost of the publi-
cation in the official newspaper of the reso-
lution awarding him his contract.
In order to preserve uniformity and to fa-
cilitate the award the Board has resolved to
receive no bid unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the committee, and a certified
check on pome bank in the city and county
of San Francisco in a sum equal to one-
tenth (1-10) of the bid deposited by the bid-
der with his bid and marie payable to the
clerk of the Board of Supervisors, conditioned
if the proposal be accepted and the contract
awarded, and If the bidder shall fall or
neglect to pay the. printing charges, execute
the contract and give the required bond with-
in six days after the award is made, then and
in that case the said sum shall be collected
and paid into the Cltv and County Treasury
by said clerk as liquidated damages for such
failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the clerk.
The Board reserves the right to reject all
bids if the public good so remilres.
JNO. A. RUSSELL, Clerk.

A MINING MAN
FIRED UPON BY
AN ASSASSIN
Walter E. Sell Shot at as He Sat
in His Dining Room in East
Berkeley.
The Bloodthirsty Visitor Missed His Mark,
but Made Good His Escape in the
Dark, Leaving Behind a Threat
ening Letter.
WALTER E. BELL, a well-known
mining man. secretary of Charles
D. Lane of the Utica mine, is
practically intrenched in his cot
tage at East Berkeley, fearful every mo
ment of hearing the crack of the revolver
of an unknown assassin. He was made
the target last Friday night for one bui
let and knows not when the next, better
aimed, may find lodgment in his body.
Sell is a man well past 60 years of ape.
};â¢ lives at 221' i Ellsworth street in a
vine-embowerrd cottage, around which
teem to linger nothing but airs of peace.
He is a well-known citizen of the com
munity and, as far as he will say. has
no enemy with a grievance sufficiently
deep to call for bloodshed. With all this
the first bullet has been sped at his life,
and. he fears, the next may reach the
mark. Hr was so prostrated by the shock
he received Friday night that he is a
sick man now. and another attempt of
the fame kind may succeed, even if the
missile flies wider from the mark than
the first.
Sell was sitting in the dining room of
his residence on the night named, talking
with a visitor, whose name cannot be ob
tained. They sat close to the table and
between it and the open window of the
dining-room, which opens into the gar
den at the side. The thought of danger
was not in tho mind of either man, when
they were startled from their Beats by the
loud report of some weapon. A bullet
â whizzed between the head of Sell and
his companion, followed by a puff of
Bmoke and a splintering noise ns the bul
let buried Itself in the woodwork of the
opposite wall above them.
For a moment neither man spoke. They
were both paralyzed from the shock, and
The Victim and the Scene of the Crime of the Unknown Assassin.
â wh<-n they did recover, the first impulse
They rushed
Into the hallway and. after a moment,
hearing no further hostile demonstration,
returned to the dining, room, which still ;
smelted r.f the smoke of the would-be
In's ri volver.
Mr. Bell, who Is tint in thÂ» most robust
of health, was Buffering greatly from the j
nd was compelled to
firop Into a chair, while his friend, a
more vigorous mat I to make
an Investigation.
On the.sill "f the window through which
the slv.t was fired was found a note ad
dressed to "Walter K. Bell." advising him
to have upon his person the sum of $'-W
would meet him and de
mand that amount. The finding of thi*
missive confirmed the suspicion of S< H
and his friend that the shot had been
fired at tli- mining man. whether or not
to kill Is yei an open quei U< a. The loud
r>!'" r '- followed bj the smoke in the
room, Indicated that the bullet had been
sped at short range. The fact, too, that
arden at the side is narrow and the
window high showed that the person who
tired th<- shot had crawled Into the gar
den while Bell and his friend were talk
ing, crept up to the window, left the note
whef> it could t>e found and diÃCharg< d
his weapon point blank.
This last fact, coupled with the leaving
of the r,ot<\ is taken by the detect! 1
whom the case was reported to Indicate
that the shooter meant to scare Sell, not
to kill him. his ultimate object being to
approach the terrified man later and bleed
him for money.
Whoever the man was and whatever h)3
motive, he left no trace behind him but
the noto. A search was made of the
fcarden after the shot had been fired, but
no sign of the unknown visitor could be
found. He had disappeared into the
night.
It was an easy thins for the shooter to
do this, and he probably figured closely
on the effect of his shot on Sell before he
fired it. Several minutes elapsed ere the
friend of the prostrated mining; man got
into the garden, and by that time the un
known miscreant had made his way into
the shadows made fry the trees in that
section of East Berkeley.
The assault was reported soon after Its
occurrence to Marshal Lloyd of Berkeley,
and later in the night he paid a visit to
the scene, but could discover no clew to
the bloodthirsty visitor. Saturday morn
ing Mr. Sell detailed the occurrence to his
attorney, John M. Whitworth. and on his
ced the matter In the hands of
letectlve agency in this city.
Since then Mr. Sell has been practically
barricaded in his home, and nightly suf
fers the most agonizing apprehensions.
He has a male companion constantly with
him, who is very careful to whom he
opens the door.
Mr. Sell's countenance and demeanor
last night betrayed the fact that he was
still suffering from the visit of last Fri
day night. Admittance was only gained
to his home after repeated ringings at
the doorbell, which were answered by a
burly man, who appeared to act as body
guard. ,
Sell seemed greatly perturbed that the
facts should be made public. In his effort
to quiet the affair he asked his caller to
accompany him around the corner, where
his attorney, John M. Whltworth, was
attending a meeting. Preceded by his
bodyguard Sell left the house. Before he
descended the steps he fastened the door
and delayed leaving until his companion
had reached the foot of the stairs. The
latter then lighted a match and by its
flare he narrowly searched the garden, as
if he half expected to rind some one hid
den among the foliage.
Bell was greatly disappointed to find j
that his attorney had left the meeting j
house, as it necessitated his going around
to Mr. Whitworth's home. Despite the.)
fai-t that the latter's house was In dark
ness and that his counsel might hay
tired, Sell boldly walked up the stairs
and loudly rang the bell. While waiting
for an answer to the ring the bodyguard
volunteered the information that he was
with Mr. Seii at the time the shot was
fired. He stated that they were seated
in the dining-room, but refrained from
saying more, as his employer looked
warnlngly at him.
Mr. Whitworth admitted his visitor to
the hallway anil the latter quickly whis
pered the reason for' his untimely visit
and asked his advice. Whitworth stated
that he was surprised that the affair had
pot out, as he know it was only known
to a few. He thought that some impru
dent person bad given out the informa
tion and he seemed to regret it exceed
ingly.
"While I don't object to having the mat
ter given to the world at this time." he
said, "yet I think to publish It now would
defeat the ends of justice. The detectives
are working on the clews that we have in
our possession and are likely to solve the
mystery shortly."
Bell thereupon positively declined to
give his version of the shooting or the
causes that led tip to it. He declared that
if he were to make any statement it
would defeat the efforts of the detectives
who were searching for the would-be
murderer.

MILK AND GORE MIXED.
Mission Toughs Engage in a Street
Broil Which Ended in Prison.
The sidewalk and a portion of the street
at Nineteenth and J-aptdge was dyed in
crimson and white yesterday afternoon.
This state of affairs was brought about
by throe toughs, who were in company
With as many young women when August
"Harry" and Lawrence Owens drove up
in a milk wagon. To see a man drive a
milk wagonâ or, in fact, perform any
manual laborâ was distasteful to the men
of leisure, so they commenced to guy the
drivers.
The young men in the wagon wouldn't
stand the insults, and as a result there
was a fight in less time than it takes to
tell it. Harry and one of the hoodlums
named Meehan started in to polish each
other's countenance off in the most ap
prove.-] pugilistic fashion. The dairyman
proved to be the better slugger and was
a h< ad" i n his opponent when
Joseph Gallagher and Edward Sullivan,
who were up to this time entertaining
the females, took a hand in the sport.
This brought Owens into the ring to as
sist his companion.
Weapons being scarce he seized a milk
can and struck Meehan over the head,
inflicting a severe scalp wound, whjch
was dressed at the City and County Hos
pital. The noise attracted the attention
of Sergeant Griffith and Officers Watts
and Robinson to the scene. The flghters
were hustled off to the Seventeenth street
station, where they were hooked for tight -
Ing "ii ill" public street. Thr women re
tire d, apparently much delighted with
their afternoon's matinee.

Two Bad Boys in Prison.
Roy Walton, aged 11, and Charles
Dougherty, aged 14 years, were booked at
the Seventeenth street station yesterday
by Detectives Graham and Fitzgerald for
a public institution. The lads have for a
long time been annoying owners of teams
in the Mission by stealing whips and
horse blankets. When arrested they had
a pair of blankets in their possession
which they had stolen but a few minutes
previously.

DECLINED TO
ASSIST BURNS
IN HIS FIGHT
Herrin Acted Without the Sanc
tion of the Southern
Pacific.
General Hubbard Says No Railroad Money
Was Used â Edward F. Searles Ar
rives After a Long
Absence.
y^-NENERAL THOMAS H. HTJBBARD, vice president of the
In Southern Pacific Company, confiims the statement of President
Collis P. Huntington that W. F. Herrin's support of the can
didacy of Dan Burns for United States Senator was without the
sanction of the Southern Pacific Company. On being interviewed
regarding the matter General Hubbard said:
"I don't think Southern Pacific influence was used in the Sen
atorial fight in California. I know no money was used, ana I don't
think any influence was used by that company. The matter was
talked over long ago, and we decided we would do nothing for
Mr. Burns. It was decided to let him make his own campaign.
"I have read of the conference between John D. Spreckels of
The Call, M. H. de Young of the Chronicle t.nd Mr. Herrin. during
which the latter stated his preference for Mr. Burns as> a Senator
ial candilata. I suppose that Mr. Herrin was expressing his in
dividual preference. He was not representing the company. I don't
consider that we have atay right .o : . + erf ere in the politics of Cali
fornia or any other State. My dea is that the company should keep
out of politics, except in case of self-defense. It has the same right
to defend itself that an individual has. I wish they had elected a
Senator. It is a pity and unfortunate that California should be rep
resented by only one Senator f the next two years."
General Huhbard arrived from Now
York yesterday afternoon on a Bpeclal
train, accompanied by Edward 1". Searles,
whose interests he represents in the
Southern Pacific Company; I "avid K.
Rruce, a friend of Mi. Searles, and E. E.
Rudd. the general's stenographer. The
party has taken up quarters at the J'al
ace Hotel. They had scarcely hern domi
ciled when W. F. Herrin and Horace G.
Platt were announced as callers.
General Hubbard keeps himself fully
posted on all matters relating to the com
pany with which he is identified, even to
the smallest details, as was demonstrated
in the course of the interview had with
him. â¢
He stated that the reorganization \
scheme of the Central Pacific had been
nearly completed, as most of the stock
of that company had been deposited with
thf> hankers, to be exchanged for Smith
ern Pacific Company shares in equal num
ber, in order to KivÂ«> ;iii the stockholders
an opportunity to cnme into the scheme

been extended until May. The so-called
Santa Fe consolidation bill, passed by the

SHAFTER TELLS AGAIN
OF TORRAL'S SURRENDER
PRESBYTERIANS GIVE HIM A
ROYAL WELCOME.
A Brilliant Programme Is Rendered
at the Van Ness Avenue
Church.
General Rhafter was obliged tr> tell j
again last evening the tale of his victory
at Santiago, and although since it all i
happened he has told it times without
number ho has become none the better
speaker. He gave hia hearers simply the
plain unvarnished tale of how it all hap
pened.
This latest recital of the story of the
brave siege of Santiago and of Torals
surrender was occasioned by a reception

THROWN BY A CABLE CAR.
Mrs. Sawkins Testifies Against the
Market-Street Company.
The trial of the suit of Ethel D. and
Thomas Sawkins for $2'V M ""> damages
against the Market Street Railway Com
pany fame up or trial yesterday before
a jury in Judge Daingerfleld's department
of the Superior Court. Linforth & Whit
aker represented the plaintiffs and Gen
eral W. M. 1.. Barnes and Daniel A. Ryan
appeared for the railroad company.
The suit grows out of an accident on
March 23, 1898, when Mrs. Sawkins, in at
tempting to hoard a Hayes-street cable
car. was thrown violently to the ground
and. she claims, seriously and permanent
ly Injured by the sudden starting of the
car.
The company does not deny that she
was so thrown, but alleges that she was
not so badly injured as she charges. It
further seeks to prove thai the 'sudden
starting of the car was the result of an
accident that could not have been forc
e een.
The following jury was sworn to try the
case; a. Hubers, Joseph Cadenasso, The
odore Conn, P. 11. Knglebert, Timothy I.
o'Hrien. M. .1. Hogan, Charles A. E<*en
nett, Adolph Asher, William Johnston,',
B. l>. Kohlberg, W. .1. Levy, Richard A.
Hourne. Only two witnesses. Ashton Ste
vens and Mrs. Sawkins. had been exam
ined when the court adjourned till 10
o'clock this morning.

Railway Employes' Entertainment.
San Francisco Club No. 1 of the Railway
Employes' Association gave its first quar
terly entertainment last evening in Na
tive Sons' building. Representatives of
every branch of the railway service in
the city to the number of more than a
thousand congregated in the banquet
hall and listened to a literary and musical
programme replete with choice selections,
which were received with much applause.
The entertainment opened with an ad
dress by D. L. Fitzgerald, president
of the club, and he was followed by H. 1,.
Banborn, chairman of the State advisory
boan] of the organization, who made a
strong appeal for* organization. At the
close of the regular programme F. V
Meyers, chairman of the grievance com
mittee, Locomotive Engineers, told of the.
great strength of the organization
throughout the State.

"The Trees of California."
Pr. W. T>. Jeppon of the University of
California delivered a lecture at the
Academy of Sciences last evening on
"The Trees of California." The profes
sor spoke on the excellent primeval f
conditions of the State, and then on the i
vast desolation which has occurred during i
the past few years. He urged that the
forestry laws be more strictly enforced
and that patrols be organised for that |
purpose.
